                                                    GLOSSARY

Affected Households (AH). Any household, physically and/or economically affected as a result
of land acquisition and involuntary resettlement. Household means all persons living and eating
together as a single social unit. They are also referred to as Project Affected Households (PAH).
When referring to an individual they are also referred to as Affected Persons (AP) or Project
Affected Person/Parties (PAP).

Associated Facilities means facilities or activities that are not funded as part of the project and,
in the judgment of the Bank, are: (a) directly and significantly related to the project; (b) carried
out, or planned to be carried out, contemporaneously with the project; and (c) necessary for
the project to be viable and would not have been constructed, expanded or conducted if the
project did not exist. For facilities or activities to be Associated Facilities, they must meet all
three criteria.

Census. A census to identify the persons who will be affected by the project, to establish an
inventory of land and assets to be affected, to determine who will be eligible for compensation
and assistance, and to discourage ineligible persons, such as opportunistic settlers, from
claiming benefits

Compensation. Payment in cash or in kind for an asset or a resource that is acquired or affected
by a project at the time the asset needs to be replaced.

Cut-off date. date of registry or census that serves as the eligible cut-off date to prevent any
further encroachers or other persons willing to take advantage of benefits. The cut-off date will
be the date of commencement of the census for each Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement
Plan.

Detailed Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement Plan. Prepared when detailed designs or
land demarcation have been completed and the full impacts following a detailed measurement
survey are known.

Displaced Persons. Any person, household or entity that is physically displaced (relocation, loss
of residential land or loss of shelter) and/or economically displaced (loss of land, assets, access
to assets, income sources or means of livelihood) as a result of (i) involuntary acquisition of
land, or (ii) involuntary restrictions on land use or on access to pastures. They are also referred
to as Affected Persons (AP) or Affected Households (AH).

Economic Loss. Loss of land, assets, access to assets, income sources, or means of livelihood as
a result of (i) involuntary acquisition of land, or (ii) involuntary restrictions on land use or on
access to pastures.

Eminent Domain. The right of GoKR using its sovereign power to acquire land for public
purposes. National law establishes which public agencies have the prerogative to exercise
eminent domain.




                                                                                                                       xi


                                                                                                                   Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement
Framework

Entitlement. Resettlement entitlements with respect to a particular eligibility category are the
sum total of compensation and other forms of assistance provided to affected persons in the
respective eligibility category.

Environment and Social Management Plan. Document detailing the environment and social
impacts of a project as well as specific mitigation measures, with detailed actions and roles and
responsibilities. The ESMP is prepared once project design is known.

Forced eviction is defined as the permanent or temporary removal against the will of
individuals, families, and/or communities from the homes and/ or land which they occupy
without the provision of, and access to, appropriate forms of legal and other protection,
including all applicable procedures and principles in ESS5. The exercise of eminent domain,
compulsory acquisition or similar powers by a Borrower will not be considered to be forced
eviction providing it complies with the requirements of national law and the provisions of ESS5,
and is conducted in a manner consistent with basic principles of due process (including
provision of adequate advance notice, meaningful opportunities to lodge grievances and
appeals, and avoidance of the use of unnecessary, disproportionate or excessive force).

Inventory of Losses (IOL) is process of accounting for physical assets, loss of pastures use, and
income affected by Project

Involuntary Resettlement. Resettlement is considered involuntary when directly affected
persons or communities do not have the right to refuse project related land acquisition or
restrictions on land use that result in their displacement.

Land acquisition. Refers to all methods of obtaining land for project purposes, which may
include outright purchase, expropriation of property and acquisition of access rights, such as
easements or rights of way. Land acquisition may also include: (a) acquisition of unoccupied or
unutilized land whether or not the landholder relies upon such land for income or livelihood
purposes; (b) repossession of public land that is used or occupied by individuals or households;
and (c) project impacts that result in land being submerged or otherwise rendered unusable or
inaccessible. “Land” includes anything growing on or permanently affixed to land, such as crops,
buildings and other improvements.

Livelihood. Refers to the full range of means that individuals, families and communities utilize
to make a living, such as wage-based income, agriculture, livestock, fishing, foraging, other
natural resource-based livelihoods, petty trade and bartering.

Livelihood restoration. Provision of assistance in addition to compensation such as land
preparation, vocational training or job opportunities for PAPs, improvement of income earning
capacity and production levels; or at least maintaining their living standards at the pre-project
level. Livelihood restoration strategy aims to provide PAPs with assistance in their efforts to
restore the livelihoods of PAPs who were impacted by permanent land acquisition or loss of
pastures.

Meaningful consultation. A process that (i) begins early in the project preparation stage and is
carried out at different stages of the project; (ii) provides timely disclosure of relevant and

                                                                                                                      xii


                                                                                                                   Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement
Framework

adequate information that is understandable and readily accessible to affected people; (iii) is
undertaken in an atmosphere free of intimidation or coercion with due regard to cultural norms
; and (iv) is gender inclusive and responsive, and tailored to the needs of disadvantaged and
vulnerable groups.

Physical Loss. Relocation, loss of residential land, or loss of shelter or building for seasonal use
as a result of (i) involuntary acquisition of land, or (ii) involuntary restrictions on land use or on
access pastures.

Relocation Assistance. Support provided to persons who are physically displaced by a project.
Relocation assistance may include transportation, food, shelter, and social services that are
provided to the displaced persons during their relocation. It may also include cash allowances
that compensate displaced persons for the inconvenience associated with resettlement and
defray the expenses of a transition to a new locale, such as moving expenses and lost work days.

Replacement Cost. Replacement cost is defined as a method of valuation yielding
compensation sufficient to replace assets, plus necessary transaction costs associated with
asset replacement. It involves replacing an asset, including land, at a cost prevailing at the time
of its acquisition. This includes fair market value, transaction costs, interest accrued, transitional
and restoration costs, and any other applicable payments, if any. Depreciation of assets and
structures should not be taken into account for replacement cost. Where there are no active
market conditions, replacement cost may be determined through alternative means, such as
cost of all building materials, labour cost for construction, and any transaction or relocation
costs. In all instances where physical displacement results in loss of shelter, replacement cost
must at least be sufficient to enable purchase or construction of housing that meets acceptable
minimum community standards of quality and safety. The valuation method for determining
replacement cost should be documented and included in relevant resettlement planning
documents. Transaction costs include administrative charges, registration or title fees,
reasonable moving expenses, and any similar costs imposed on affected persons. To ensure
compensation at replacement cost, planned compensation rates may require updating in
project areas where inflation is high or the period of time between calculation of compensation
rates and delivery of compensation is extensive.

Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement Plan. Resettlement Plan (LRRP) is a resettlement
instrument (document) to be prepared during identification of sub-project sites. The LRRP
contains specific and legally binding requirements which should be met for resettlement and
compensation to an affected party prior to implementation of a project activity which caused
an adverse impact.

Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement Framework. Livelihood Restoration and
Resettlement Framework (RLRF) is an instrument to be used throughout the project. The LRRF
sets out resettlement objectives and principles, administrative arrangements and funding
mechanisms for any Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement measures that may be needed
during the project implementation. The LRRF guides the preparation of livelihood restoration
and resettlement plans for site-specific activities to meet the needs of the persons who may be
affected by the project.


                                                                                                                     xiii


                                                                                                                   Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement
Framework

Restrictions on land use. Refers to limitations or prohibitions on the use of pasture, agricultural,
residential, commercial or other land that are directly introduced and put into effect as part of
the project. These may include restrictions on access to pastures, restrictions on access to other
common property resources, and restrictions on land use within utility easements or safety
zones.

Security of tenure. Means that resettled individuals or communities are resettled to a site that
they can legally occupy, where they are protected from the risk of eviction and where the
tenure rights provided to them are socially and culturally appropriate.

Vulnerable or disadvantaged households. Refers to those who may be more likely to be
adversely affected by the project impacts and/or more limited than others in their ability to
take advantage of a project’s benefits Such an individual/group is also more likely to be
excluded from/unable to participate fully in the mainstream consultation process and as such
may require specific measures and/or assistance to do so. This will take into account
considerations relating to gender, age, including the elderly and minors. Vulnerable groups are
defined in Art. 4 of the Law of the Kyrgyz Republic of December 19, 2001 No. 111 "On the
Fundamentals of Social Services to the Population in the Kyrgyz Republic “Socially vulnerable
population groups under the project may include the following categories, among others: 1)
Low-income households and individuals; 2) The elderly in ; 3) People with disabilities; 4) Women
headed low-income households.
World Bank’s Environmental and Social Standards. The ESSs set out requirements for
Borrowers/Clients to identify and assess environmental and social risks and impacts associated
with projects supported by the Bank through Investment Project Financing. The 10 ESSs set out
standards that the Borrower and the project should meet throughout the project The ESSs are
designed to avoid, minimize, reduce or mitigate the adverse environmental and social risks and
impacts of the Project.

Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement
Framework


                                        EXECUTIVE SUMMARY
Introduction
1.     The proposed Kambarata-1 Hydro Power Plant Sustainable and Transformational
Energy Program (K1-STEP) supports the GoKR and Central Asia countries to sustain strong
economic growth, enhance energy security and advance the clean energy transition. The K1-
STEP could play a major role in meeting the country’s renewable energy targets, facilitating
renewable energy integration, and mitigating climate change impacts.
2.      The Kambarata-1 Hydro Power Plant (K1-HPP) is located in the middle reaches of the
Naryn River, 14.5 km upstream of the existing Kambarata-2-HPP in the Toktogul and Toguz-Toro
districts of Jalal-Abad Region. It is located in a gorge where the River makes a large loop around
the Karabash Mountain. The dam will be a roller-compacted concrete (RCC) gravity dam, 10 m
wide at the crest and has a maximum height above the riverbed of 256 m.
3.      The reservoir area will be 61.7 km2 at a full supply level (FSL) of 1,198 m above sea level
(masl). The inundated area will extend approximately 68 km upstream from the dam wall. As
per Kyrgyz regulations on water bodies, a water protection zone (WPZ) of 500 m around the
reservoir will be established once the reservoir is inundated and in use.
4.     The use of multipurpose dam infrastructure, such as K1-HPP, will increasingly play an
important role in Central Asia towards improved water management, climate resilience and
carbon-neutral energy production.
Scope of Land Acquisition, Resettlement and Livelihood Impacts
5.     Construction of the dam will cause permanent irreversible impacts due to the creation
of the reservoir, headrace tunnel, powerhouse and source of livelihoods is livestock farming
(sheep, goats, cattle and horses), which is highly dependent on land and natural pass. Loss of
pastures will lead to significant economic displacement of low-income rural communities in the
reservoir and WPZ areas.
6.       It is estimated that 1,024 PAPs (1,021 pastures users and 3 shepherds1) equivalent to
6,211 persons will be affected by the loss of 3,538.5 ha of pastures in the inundated zone. Land
belonging or used by one forestry enterprise, Toktogul, and one hunting company, Zhoon Terek,
will also be affected. Beekeeping is also a small yet significant livelihood activity, practiced by a
limited number of households, that will be affected. Loss of access may also cause additional
loss of pastures if access to these pastures cannot be restored. Restrictions of land use in the
WPZ may also affect pasture users; an estimated area of 4,683 ha of pastures is located within
the WPZ. Few structures (54) will be affected in the reservoir area; they are mainly temporary
shelters (41) and secondary structures (13) used by livestock owners when they bring their
livestock for grazing. Three (3) households will have to be physically displaced as they reside
permanently in the inundated areas. 13 structures are also located in the WPZ. Eight (8) graves
and two (2) cultural sites will also be affected as well as salt extraction site. A number of crops
and trees will also be affected.




1
 These 3 shepherds are responsible for collecting and grazing cattle and sheep from 22 AHs across the villages of
Birdik, Aral, and Kazarman

                                                                                                                      xv


                                                                                                                   Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement
Framework

Purpose of the Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement Framework (LRRF)
7.     This Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement Framework (LRRF) details the policies,
procedures, and processes to identify and mitigate impacts on people who will be affected by
physical and economic displacement due to the construction and operation of the Project. The
LRRF has been developed in accordance with regulations of the Kyrgyz Republic and the World
Bank’s Environmental and Social Standard 5 (ESS 5) on “Land Acquisition, Restrictions on Land
Use, and Involuntary Resettlement.”
8.      This LRRF will guide the preparation of the future Livelihood Restoration and
Resettlement Plans (LRRPs) under the Project. Two Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement
Plans (LRRPs) will be prepared: 1) Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement Plan 1, covering the
construction sites, early works (mobilization of early works contractor, access roads to left bank,
bridge to powerhouse, bridge to right bank, access roads to right bank, access road to river
diversion inlet and outlet structures, camp and workshop for early works contract, 2 no.
diversion tunnels including inlets and outlets, excavation of dam left and right abutments down
to river level, excavation of power intake structures down to 1125 masl, excavation of
powerhouse and substation area to 966.4 masl.) and early works/associated facilities , will be
prepared at the beginning of project implementation and 2) Livelihood Restoration and
Resettlement Plan 2, covering the reservoir impounded area and the WPZ, will be prepared
later during project construction.
9.     Given that resettlement activities are being carried out in a phased manner, to
correspond to impoundment levels of the reservoir, as well as the magnitude of people affected
by economic displacement, a framework approach to identify and mitigate adverse impacts on
Project Affected Persons (PAP) was considered appropriate. The LRRF also leaves flexibility to
plan the scheduling of cut-off dates, census and valuation at the right time in project
development and for the preparation of livelihood restoration and resettlement plans.
Information Disclosure, Consultation and Participation
10.     During LRRF/ESIA preparation a first round of consultations was conducted in October
and November 2024. These consultations took place at national level (one meeting, 35
participants including 9 NGOs) and at local level (5 meetings, 213 participants from all 10
affected villages). Key Informant Interviews (KII) were also conducted with local authorities,
pastures committee, schools and health heads. In addition, a total of 19 Focus Groups
Discussions (FGDs) were conducted (7 with women, 5 with local authorities and 7 with herders).

11.    A second round of consultation was conducted in October and November 2025 at
national level (two meetings including one meeting with riparian countries) and at local level (5
meetings, 480 participants) to present the results of the environmental and social studies.The
purpose of these consultations was to explain the project, to describe the anticipated social and
environmental impacts and to let all interested parties ask questions, express their concerns,
and provide suggestions on the Project. All the suggestions, opinions and concerns raised by
the meetings/FGDs/KII participants have been addressed and incorporated into this LRRF.
12.    A Stakeholder Engagement Plan (SEP) has also been prepared. Going forward, as
described in the SEP and this LRRF, gender and vulnerability aspects will be integrated into the
consultation activities.



                                                                                                                     xvi


                                                                                                                   Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement
Framework

13.     In order to make the LRRF/LRRPs preparation and implementation process transparent,
Executive Summary and salient features of LRRF shall be translated in local Kyrgyz language and
disclosed on the MoE, Implementing Agencies’ websites, site offices for wider dissemination.
Eligibility and entitlements
14.       People directly affected by the project and eligible to compensation or support are:
15.    Persons using agricultural and/or pasture land that will be permanently affected by the
project or losing access to those lands;
      -   Persons whose livelihood will be affected (permanently or temporarily) by the Project
          (pastures users i.e. livestock herders, shepherds and beekeepers; persons engaged in
          the collection of forest products etc.);
      -   Persons whose other non-land assets (including graves) are affected by the Project or
          losing access to those assets in part or in total by the Project;
16.    Under this Project, the cut-off dates for eligibility for entitlements will be the dates of
commencement of the census for each Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement Plan (LRRP).
Two cut off dates will be established: i) one for the construction sites, early works and
associated facilities and ii) one for the reservoir impounded area and the WPZ . These cut-of
dates will be disclosed in each LRRP and locally.
17.    PAPs will be notified well in advance of relocation to provide necessary time to plan and
relocate their assets/livestock with the support of K1-PMO and local authorities. The
entitlement matrix will be shared with all affected communities, translated in Kyrgyz language,
which will be attached to the LRRPs. As detailed in the SEP, these entitlements will be
communicated through various means, including national media sources, local communication
channels, T social media (WhatsApp) and printed leaflets.
18.     Livelihood restoration. A livelihood restoration program will be included as part of the
Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement Plan (LRRP) will be implemented to provide PAPs with
assistance in their efforts to improve, or at least restore, their incomes and standards of living
after they have been displaced.
19.     All pasture users located in the inundated area or losing access to these pastures will be
eligible to benefit from this LRRP. As a priority, other pastures of the same category (i.e. winter,
spring-autumn and summer) and in the same area, will be allocated to affected pasture users.
However, when pastures cannot be replaced as no other similar quality pastures are available
in the area, other income restoration measures will be developed. The final LRRP will be part of
the LRRP2 for the inundated area.
20.     A Grievance Redress Mechanism (GRM) to handle resettlement related complaints will
be established. This GRM will be a two stages process: i) First, at local level. At the village level
(Aiyl Okmotu) local contact persons receive/register grievances from applicants, notify the
responsible K-1 PMO GRM focal person and propose solutions to solve grievance. If the
applicant is not satisfied and/or the issue cannot be resolved at the first level, the complaint is
escalated to the second (central) level; ii) At the second (central) level, the K1-PMO responsible
staff receives and registers the complaint; they will review the complaint and make a decision.
The applicant may terminate participation in the project GRM process at any time and seek
resolution through the courts in accordance with the legislation of the Kyrgyz Republic.

                                                                                                                   Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement

Implementation Arrangements,
21.     For the implementation of the Kambarata 1 HPP, the Ministry of Energy of the Kyrgyz
Republic has established a Project Management Office (K1-PMO). K1-PMO is responsible for
assigning appropriate staff and budget, sufficient to implement this LRRF and prepare and
implement, as needed, the LRRPs. Qualified consultants may be mobilized to assist K1-PMO in
development of the LRRPs. The LRRPs implementation will also require the participation of
national, regional and local agencies and organizations.
22.    It is suggested to establish an Inter-Agency Commission on Resettlement (IACR) to
address all resettlement and livelihood issues. This agency will include all relevant government
agencies. This agency will be established prior to the LRRPs process being started.
23.     Given that livelihood restoration support is vital, K1-PMO will need to be supported
with additional capacity and ensure continuous and effective coordination with MoE so that
affected people are aware of the livelihood restoration process, and the livelihood support
opportunities and entitlements that the Government of Kyrgyzstan is providing.


Monitoring
24.     The K1-PMO will be in charge of conducting the internal monitoring of the LRRPs
implementation. The internal monitoring is to (i) ensure that the standard of living of PAPs are
restored or improved; (ii) monitor whether overall subproject, resettlement objectives are
being met; (iii) assess if livelihood restoration measures and compensation/support are
sufficient; (iv) identify outstanding issues or risks; and (v) identify measures to mitigate the
problems. The K1-PMO will submit semi-annual safeguards monitoring reports to the WB.
25.   An external monitoring agency (EMA) will also be hired by the K1-PMO to conduct
independent monitoring and will submit quarterly reports to the K1-PMO and the WB.
Budget

492,673
    28. Costs for the Livelihood Restoration Plan implementation: USD 1,868,981.

30.     Costs for independent monitoring: USD 50,000
31.     Costs for preparation of 2 LRRPs: 330,000
32.     Contingency costs (10%): USD 474,165

3 INTRODUCTION

3.1 Background

1.     The Kyrgyz Republic is a landlocked, lower-middle-income country with rich natural
endowments, including minerals, forests, arable land and pastures, and significant potential
for expansion of agriculture, hydroelectricity production, and tourism.
2.      In October 2021, the Government of Kyrgyz Republic launched a medium-term
development program for 2021 to 2026 with the goal of “leaving no one behind.” The priority
areas of the program include anti-crisis measures; management reform; establishment of an
enabling environment for development; development of key sectors of the economy,
including energy; social development; foreign policy and national security; and special
priorities. Energy is presented as a key sector to be reformed, including through gradual tariff
adjustments toward cost recovery, exploiting the country’s renewable energy potential,
modernization of sector assets, and transition to the wholesale electricity market.
3.      In the recently prepared Energy White Paper that lays out the pillars of reform
direction, the Government has set ambitious targets for renewable energy deployment.
Under the third pillar of the white paper, the Government seeks to procure 700 megawatts
of solar capacity and as much as 100 megawatts of small hydropower plant capacity by the
end of 2030, and it has accelerated the preparation of large HPPs such as Kambarata-1, which
would take a decade to develop and construct, to support the ambitious transformation of
the energy sector.
4.     The proposed Kambarata-1 Hydro Power Plant Sustainable and Transformational
Energy Program (K1-STEP) supports the GoKR and Central Asia countries to sustain strong
economic growth, enhance energy security and advance the clean energy transition. The K1-
STEP could play a major role in meeting the country’s renewable energy targets, facilitating
renewable energy integration, and mitigating climate change impacts.
5.      The Kambarata-1 Hydropower Plant (K1-HPP) is a transformational greenfield clean
energy project with substantial domestic and regional benefits aimed at bolstering energy
security, accelerating the clean energy transition, improving water management and
strengthening the regional cooperation in Central Asia. The proposed plant’s installed
capacity of 1,860 megawatts could reach an average annual generation of 5,640 gigawatt
hours, which is equivalent to 1,073 megawatts of coal-/gas-fired power plants operating at a
60 percent capacity factor. It is estimated to avoid emission of about 4.8 million tons of CO2
per year from coal plants or about 2.5 million tons of CO2 from gas power plants.
6.     The K1-STEP could also address the country’s energy security challenge, meeting
export obligations and realizing export revenues, while improving downstream HPP
operations and water management. The development of renewables will greatly reduce the
country’s reliance on imported fossil fuels in winter while providing the country with
additional export revenue.
7.     The K1-STEP is being prepared under the World Bank’s Environment and Social
Framework (ESF) and a Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement Framework (LRRF) need to
be prepared (see section 1.4).




                                                                                                                   1
                                                                                                                Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework



3.2 Project Components
8.     The K1-STEP Program is designed in three phases with indicative total funding
envelope of US$1,500 million, including the Phase 1 US$500 million, from the World Bank to
the Kyrgyz Republic, the Republic of Uzbekistan, and the Republic of Kazakhstan. Phase 1 of
the K1-STEP Program will consist of the following components.
    • Component 1: Early works. This component will finance the early works such as
        diversion tunnels, dam, powerhouse and substation pre-excavation, site installation.
    • Component 2. Project design, management and supervision. This component will
        finance the Project Management Consultancy (PMC), project implementation
        support, and the operationalization of the Kambarata-1 Regional JV.
         a) Subcomponent 1. Operationalization of the K-1 Regional project company.
         b) Subcomponent 2. Project Management Consultancy (PMC).
         c) Subcomponent 3. Project implementation support.
         d) Subcomponent 4. Owner’s cost.
9.     Component 3. Implementation of social and environmental management plans. This
component will finance the implementation and monitoring of E&S instruments including
ESMP, LRRF and Livelihood Restoration Plan (LRP) requirements. This component will also
support the implementation of a comprehensive benefit sharing plan in support of possible
affected communities and national social programs.
    • Component 4. Water management institutional capacity and regional coordination
        support. This component will finance a set of hydro meteorological instrumentation
        and consultancy services required for the K1-HPP project, including key monitoring
        investments in the upper Naryn river basin to support the operation of Kambarata-1
        HPP and mitigate future climate risks, and improve the water management
        institutional foundations within the K-1 HPP project and broader regional framework.

3.3 Project Features
10.   The K1-HPP was developed back in the Soviet years. As planned, the K1-HPP will
become the largest hydroelectric power station in Kyrgyzstan with 1,860 megawatts. The
proposed site for the K-1 HPP is located in the middle reaches of the Naryn River, about 14.5
km upstream of the existing Kambarata-2 HPP dam (see Figures 1 and 2).

3.4 Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement Framework (LRRF)
13.    During detailed design, there may be new investments or adjustments to the proposed
investments. Therefore, as the design is not finalized and all impacts cannot be accurately
assessed at the time of appraisal, a Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement Framework
(LRRF) will be prepared, and site-specific plans will be prepared and implemented after the
project is approved.
14.     The LRRF also leaves flexibility to plan the scheduling of cut-off dates, census and
valuation at the right time in project development and for the preparation of livelihood
restoration and resettlement plans. The preparation process and sequence of resettlement
planning documents are the following.
15.    Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement Framework, prepared at current stage,
during project preparation.
16.    Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement Plan 1, covering the construction sites, early
works and associated facilities, will be prepared at the beginning of project implementation.
17.    Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement Plan 2, covering the reservoir impounded
area and the water protection zone, will be prepared before the dam is completed.
18.     This LRRF is prepared based on the World Bank’s Environmental and Social Framework
(ESF) and the Kyrgyz Republic’s relevant laws and regulations. The objective of this LRRF is to
establish resettlement principles, organizational arrangements, funding mechanisms, and
design criteria to be applied to projects to be prepared during project implementation. This
LRRF will be applied to all activities of this project, early works and associated facilities that
result in involuntary resettlement, land use restrictions and livelihood impacts regardless of
the source of financing.
19.    The basic objective of the LRRF is to ensure that all Project Affected Parties (PAPs) will
be timely compensated at replacement cost and assisted with restoration measures to help
them improve or at least maintain their livelihoods and living standards, in real terms, to pre-
displacement levels or to levels prevailing prior to the beginning of project implementation,
whichever is higher. The LRRF is prepared to guide the preparation of LRRPs during the project
implementation phase. The LRRF gives out bases for preparing, reviewing and approval of
LRRPs for the projects that require land acquisition, resettlement support and livelihood
restoration regardless of financial sources.
20.    This LRRF is to be submitted to the Ministry of Energy (MoE) management for approval
and endorsed by the World Bank before Appraisal. The LRRF will be disclosed in Russian and
Kyrgyz languages and made available on the project website and in the project area for
affected communities. The English version of the LRRF will be publicly disclosed at the World
Bank internal and external website.
21.    A Resettlement Framework has been prepared in March 2023 for the Kyrgyzstan
Renewable Energy Development Project (KRED) Phase 1 funded by the WB. This project
included the K1-HPP but also small and medium-scale hydropower plants and solar power
projects. This LRRF supersedes the RF developed in 2023 under the KRED regarding K1-HPP.

3.5 Project Beneficiaries
22.  Beneficiaries of the K1-STEP are the electricity consumers, including industrial,
commercial, and residential customers. The newly constructed hydropower plant under this


                                                                                                                   4
                                                                                                                Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework


phase will help increase power supply in the Kyrgyz Republic and improve the quality of power
in the country. In addition, the local population is expected to benefit to a certain extent from
employment opportunities during the construction and operation phases. The implementing
agencies will also benefit significantly from the capacity building and technical assistance they
will receive, which will improve their capacity to plan, develop, manage and maintain existing
hydropower systems and services. Consequently, they will be able to deliver better, more
reliable, and cost-effective services to customers.

    4 GEOGRAPHICAL OVERVIEW
    25.     The K1 HPP area is located in the middle reaches of the Naryn River, 14.5 km upstream of the
    existing K2-2 HPP dam in the Toktogul District of Jalal-Abad Region. It is located in a gorge where the
    River makes a large loop around the Karabash Mountain (see Figures 2 and 3).
    4.1 Source of Information
    26.     Sources of information for this chapter come from the literature review, Key Informant
    Interviews (KII), Focus Groups Discussions (FGDs) and household surveys conducted as part of the
    ESIA/SIA preparation at the end of 2024 and in early 2025. KIIs were conducted among local
    authorities, pasture committees and health and education leaders. FGDs (19) were also conducted (7
    with women, 5 with local authorities and 7 with herders). The survey was conducted among 337 HHs.
    The survey covered 13 villages across four AAs (12 villages located in Toktogul district and one in
    Toguz-Toro district). Documents prepared under the project were also used for this report2.
    4.2 Administrative Divisions
    27.      The administrative-territorial division in Kyrgyzstan comprises seven regions (oblast) and two
    cities (Bishkek city, the capital of the country, and Osh city, the second largest city in the country).
    28.      Regions are divided into districts (rayons). In turn, a district is divided into town and rural
    communities (Aiyl Aimak). The Aiyl Aimak (AA) plays a crucial role in Kyrgyzstan's administrative
    structure, especially at the local level, as it is the primary administrative unit for rural areas. Each AA
    consists of several villages or settlements (Aiyl Okmutu) which are collectively governed by an
    appointed local administration known as Akim, who is responsible for local governance and
    facilitating the implementation of state policies at the grassroots level. Figure 4 provides a
    summarised presentation of the overall administrative structure of the Kyrgyz Republic
                               Figure 4: Administrative Structure of the Kyrgyz Republic
          National Level
          •President → Prime Minister and Cabinet → Ministries
           The national government & ministries oversee the country, setting policies, regulations, and goals

          Regional Level (Oblast)
          •Akim of Oblast (Regional Governor) → Regional Departments (Health, Education, Agriculture, Infrastructure)
           The Akim of the Oblast (appointed by the President) manages the implementation of national policies and
           oversees the administrative units (Rayons) in the region.

          District Level (Rayon)
          •Akim of Rayon (District Governor) → District Adm. Offices (Health, Education, Agriculture, Infrastructure).
           The Akim of the Rayon (appointed by the Akim of the Oblast) is responsible for managing public services and
           coordinating development within the district.
          Local Governance
          •Aiyl Aimak and Village (Aiyl Okmutu):
           Akim of Aiyl Aimak → Kenesh (Local Council) → Village Administration
           Akim of Village → Village-Level Services (Education, Health, Infrastructure)
          •Urban Local Governance (Cities):
           Akim of the City → City Administration Offices (Urban Planning, Housing, Transportation, etc.)
           City Kenesh (City Council) → City-Level Services (Education, Health, Infrastructure, Public Transport)

30.     Toktogul district. Toktogul district covers an area of 7,815 km2 the district had a recorded
population of 108,938 as of 2025. The district consists of one city (Toktogul), 10 rural communities
(Aiyl Aimak), and 49 villages.

31.     It should be noted that since 2024, Kyrgyzstan has begun implementing a large-scale
administrative-territorial reform3 aimed at consolidating and optimizing rural districts and cities. As
part of the reform, the number of existing rural districts was decided to be reduced from 452 to 235
consolidated rural districts. Under this reform, in Toktogul District, Nichke-Say AA was combined with
Kyzyl-Ozgorush AA to become Elmirbek Imanaliev Aiyl Aimak (AA).
32.      The inundated area will affect ten (10) villages located in two districts as described below.
33.      Sary- Kamysh (Toktogul district): Sary-Kamish AA, located on the right side of the Naryn river,
is one of the smallest administrative units within Toktogul District, comprising just two villages (Birlik
and Kotoromo) with a total population of approximately 1,530 by the beginning of 2024.
34.      Toluk (Toktogul district): Toluk AA, located on the right side of the Naryn river, is a small
administrative unit within Toktogul District, consisting of only four villages with a total population of
approximately 3,332 (2024).
    35. Elmirbek Imanaliev (Toktogul district): Elmirbek Imanaliev AA, located on the left side of the
         Naryn river, is the largest AA within Toktogul District, consisting of 12 villages, of which only
         2 villages will be affected by K1 HPP: Nichke-Sai and Chorogun. By the beginning of 2024, the
         AA had a total population of 15,064 making it a prominent community in the district.
36.      Atai (Toguz-Toro district): Toguz-Toro District is part of the Jalal-Abad Region in western
Kyrgyzstan, with its administrative center located in Kazarman. The district spans an area of 3,816
km2 and had a resident population of 28,581 as of 2025. The district comprises 14 settlements
organized into 5 AAs. Within the district, only two villages Atai AA (Atai and Aral villages) will be
slightly affected by the inundated area.
4.3 Natural Conditions
4.3.1 Topography
37.     The K-1 HPP site is situated at an average altitude of 1,000 meters above sea level along the
Naryn River. The topography of the proposed K1 HPP is mostly shaped by the Naryn river. Originating
primarily from the glaciers and snowfields of the Akshiirak mountain range of central Tien-Shan, the
Naryn River cuts through the spurs of the Fergana and Chaak-Too ridges and within the Jalal-Abad
region.
38.     The planned Kambarata-1 reservoir is located in the Karabash Gorge, which is more than 150
km long (See Figure 6). The gorge is a V-shaped valley deeply incised into granite formations. The
gorge's steep and, in some sections, vertical walls rise over 300 meters above the riverbed, providing
a natural channel that facilitates dam construction. Within the gorge, the Naryn shows a high degree
of confinement, without extensive floodplains.
4.3.2 Climate
39.     Jalal-Abad region is included in the climatic region of South-Western Kyrgyzstan, which is the
warmest and most humid region of the republic. Unlike other areas, during the cold season, a
significant amount of precipitation falls here under the influence of southern cyclones.
40.     The region experiences a sharply continental climate, marked by significant temperature
variations between seasons. Winters are cold and harsh, while summers are warm and relatively dry.
The climatic conditions of the Toktogul district are characterized by the following data: the average
air temperature in January is -8°C in the valley part, -12°C in the mountainous part. In July, the average
monthly temperatures are +26°C in the valley and +8°C in the mountains. Minimum air temperature

3

Reform in Pilot Mode at the Level of Aiyl Aimaks and Cities of the Kyrgyz Republic

                                                                                                              Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program     Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework
minus 38°C, maximum +38°C. The average annual precipitation is 400 mm in the valleys and 400-600
mm in the mountainous parts of the region. The daily maximum precipitation can reach 40 mm in the
valley and up to 70 mm in the mountains4.

4.3.3 Water resources
41.       The project area is located within the Naryn river valley. The Naryn is the largest river in the
country, with a maximum discharge of 2,880 m³/s. Upstream of the Kambarata-2 HPP, the Naryn is
still a free-flowing river. The Naryn merges with the Kara Darya to form the Syr Darya, the largest
tributary of the Aral Sea. The water resources of the Kyrgyz rivers are crucial also for the downstream
populations in Uzbekistan and Kazakhstan5.
42.     The Naryn River is fed by the Kok-Irim, Kempir-Ata, Nichke-Sai and other rivers on the left, and
by the Toluk, Torken, Chychkan, Uzun-Akmat, Kara-Suu and other rivers on the right. These rivers
valleys are deeply cut; the river currents are turbulent and there are many rapids.
43.     The Naryn River is already blocked by the five large dams of the Toktogul cascade, by
Kambarata-2 dam which has been in place since late 2009 and by Atbashy HPP located further
upstream, on a tributary of the Naryn River. The project area also includes the Toktogul reservoir,
which is currently among the largest reservoirs in the region.
4.3.4 Ecological Context
44.  The Project site is located on the Naryn River, surrounded by the western Tian Shan
Mountains. The terrain is characterized by high gorges through which the Naryn flows, high snow-



4
 Kyrgyzstan Renewable Energy Development Project; Environmental and Social Management Framework (2023)
5
  Florian Betz, Akylbek Chymyrov, Magdalena Lauermann, Bernd Cyffka & Dogdurbek Chontoev (2022) Assessing the
Impacts on Natural Ecosystems of the Future Kambarata-1 HPP on the Naryn River: A GIS-based Approach
                                                                                                                   11

                                                                                                           Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program        Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework
clad mountains with valleys covered by grassland, pastureland and arable land. Patches of shrublands
and forests can be observed in the valleys.
45.    As per the One Earth biogeographical framework and WWF Terrestrial Ecoregions of the
World, the Project Site is situated in the Tian Shan Foothill Arid Steppe''6 and the "Gissaro-Alai Open
Woodlands"7 ecoregion in the "Tian Shan-Pamir Grasslands, Mountain Steppe & Conifer Forests
(PA31)" bioregion. The Tian Shan Foothill Arid Landscape ecoregion is a complex series of ridges, lake
basins and river valleys that drain into the Aral Sea. The foothills are rich in plant species, with arid,
semi-desert vegetation to the west and desert habitat to the east. River channels and floodplains of
the foothill region, where less disturbed, hold groves and savannah woodlands of desert poplar. In
the Gissaro-Alai Open Woodlands ecoregion, vast number of tulips flower in the spring season. The
vegetation in this ecoregion consists of coniferous evergreen Juniper species and ephemeral herb
vegetation, with unique fruit and nut forests.
46.      The project area contains natural and modified habitats. Natural habitats dominate with
grasslands, shrublands, forests and patches of rocky areas and cold deserts. Modified habitats include
arable lands, which form a small part of the Study Area. Aquatic habitats consist of the inland wetland
i.e. the Naryn River.
47.     The belt of steppe vegetation covers vast areas, occupying high lands and partly lowlands in
the range of 900 – 1,300 m above sea level. These areas of meadow-steppes, which occupy large
areas, are mainly used as late spring and summer pastures. Smooth slopes and flat watersheds of this
belt are used for rainfed crops.
48.     The subalpine belt occupies a significant mountainous part of the region and is at the altitudes
of 2,000 - 3,000 m. Subalpine meadows and forest vegetation consisting of spruce, fir, juniper, maple,
birch, and etc., are widespread here. Subalpine meadows are good summer pastures with duration
of use up to four months8.

4.4 Human Environment
4.4.1 Demographic Characteristics
49.    The Jalal-Abad Region is the fourth largest province by territory and the second largest by
population in Kyrgyzstan. The population of the Jalal-Abad Region in 2025 was 1,358,534 inhabitants
and the population of the Toktogul and Toguz-Toro districts respectively 108,938 and 28,581
inhabitants9.
50.     Most of the population in the project area lives in rural area. In Toktogul district, around
80.1% of the population lives in rural areas while the urban population is around 19.9%. The Project
site area has a low population density and dispersed settlements. The population in the surroundings
of the inundated area is estimated at around 13,000 people. The settlements are not located along
the Naryn River. The table below presents the main demographic features of the affected villages.
51.    According to the household survey, approximately 51.3% of the surveyed households have 5
to 7 family members; 34.1% of households have 1 to 4 family members; 11% have 8 to 9 family
members; and 3.6% have more than 10 family members

4.4.2 Ethnic minorities
52.    Kyrgyzstan is a diverse, multiethnic country where people of various religions coexist
harmoniously. According to the 2022 census, Kyrgyzstan's population is ethnically diverse, with Kyrgyz
making up the majority with 77.6%; other groups include: Uzbeks 14.2%, Russians 4.1%, Dungans
1.0%, Uyghurs 0.5%, and others 2.7%.
53.    According to the Initial Environmental Examination (IEE) prepared for the Toktogul dam
Rehabilitation Phase 3 Project, after the collapse of the Soviet Union the ethnic situation in Djalal-
Abad Oblast has dramatically changed. Due to its location near Uzbekistan, the Oblast is characterized
by the presence of many ethnic and language minorities. A lot of Russians, Tatars, Germans, Koreans
and other ethnic formations have left the country for Russia, Europe and America causing a change
in the ethnic composition of the local community: now dominating ethnic groups are Kyrgyz and
Uzbeks.
54.    The Toktogul and Toguz-Toro districts are home to a predominantly Kyrgyz population, with
small representations of other ethnic groups such as Uzbeks and Russians. The Aiyl Aimaks, in the
project area, are situated on the traditional lands of the Kyrgyz people, where the centuries-old
occupations and way of life of the inhabitants have fostered an ethnically homogeneous population.
55.     . According to the Socio-economic passports of the affected AAs, in the population of the
villages within the affected area, 100% of the residents identify themselves as Kyrgyz with Kyrgyz
being the predominant language spoken10.. Ethnic minorities is therefore not an issue in the Project
area.
56.     In rural areas, Kyrgyz remains the primary and preferred language, functioning as the main
mother tongue. It is often regarded as more straightforward to learn than Russian, due to its simpler
vocabulary and more accessible grammatical structure. Though most of the people of the villages in
the project area are also quite familiar with Russian language.

4.4.3 Vulnerability
•        Poverty
57.     The poverty data for the project AAs of E. Imanaliev, Toluk, Sary-Kamysh and Atai is
summarized in Error! Reference source not found.Table 4. Families are categorized based on their
income levels. There are disparities in poverty levels across the AAs. The poverty rate is respectively
of 27.2% in E. Imanaliev AA, 39.6% in Sary-Kamysh AA, 42.9% in Toluk AA and 41.7% in Atai AA. Toluk
and Atai AAs have the highest percentage of poor families, but most are just above the extreme
poverty threshold. In contrast, Sary-Kamysh and E. Imanaliev are marked by a high proportion of
families living in extreme poverty.
- Sary-Kamysh AA has 80 poor HHs, representing 39.6% of its total 202 households. A significant
  portion (36.1%) is categorized as extremely poor, with only 1.9% close to the poverty line and 1.4%
  in the poor category.
- In Toluk AA, 270 out of 630 HHs (42.8%) are categorized as poor HHs, making it the AA with the
  highest poverty level among all the AAs in the study. Among these, 191 families (30.3%) are close
  to the poverty line, 6.3% are considered poor, while 6.2% fall under the extremely poor category.
- In E. Imanaliev AA, among 2,384 HHs, 27.2% (648) are considered poor. Of these, 5.9% are near
  the poverty line (with incomes between 1,648 to 2,939 soms), 13.6% are classified as poor (with
  incomes between 1,000 to 1,648 soms), and 80.6% are extremely poor, earning less than 1,000
  soms.
- Atai AA has 512 poor HHs (41.7%) out of 1,227 total households. Of these, 261 families (51.0%)
  are close to the poverty line, while 16.6% are poor, and 25.8% are extremely poor.

4.4.4 Gender Issues
59.    FGDs with women, conducted in seven (7) out of 13 11, villages revealed a wide range of
women’s concerns, from economic vulnerabilities and lack of employment opportunities to the risks
posed by displacement and inadequate infrastructure as described below.
      - Livelihood and Economic Challenges: Women in all the villages expressed severe economic
        insecurities, primarily due to their dependence on livestock and small-scale agriculture. Many
        women actively participate in dairy production, poultry farming, and handicrafts, but these
        activities provide limited income and are seasonal in nature. Women in Kyzyl-Ozgorush and
        Nichke-Sai pointed out that they engage in sewing businesses, but the lack of markets and
        transportation hinders their ability to expand. Women in Noot Chaar-Tash emphasized that
        their seasonal migration with livestock is crucial for income. Across all FGDs, job opportunities
        for women were reported as extremely limited, and there were demands for vocational
        training, microfinance programs, and government-supported employment initiatives.
      - Water Scarcity and Drinking Water Issues: Women in all villages voiced serious concerns over
        water shortages for both domestic & agricultural use. In Kyzyl-Ozgorush and Noot Chaar-Tash,
        women reported that many HHs rely on untreated river water, leading to frequent water-borne
        diseases among children. In Toluk, the existing water pipelines are old and dysfunctional.
      - Health & Medical Services Gaps: Women in all villages raised concerns about inadequate
        healthcare services. In Toluk Almaluu and Nichke-Sai, there are no maternity facilities or full-
        time doctors, forcing women to travel long distances for childbirth and urgent medical care. In
        Aral and Birlik, women reported a lack of female doctors, which discourages them from seeking
        timely medical assistance. Across all FGDs, water contamination was identified as a major
        health risk, with many women citing previous outbreaks of hepatitis & gastrointestinal diseases.
      - Education and Childcare Facilities: Women across all villages stressed the lack of adequate
        schools and kindergartens. In Kyzyl-Ozgorush and Kotormo, school infrastructure is
        overcrowded, and some children have to walk several kilometres to attend school. Women in
        Nichke-Sai and Toluk Almaluu expressed the need for kindergartens, as many mothers struggle
        to balance childcare with work.
      - Infrastructure and Road Accessibility Issues: Women in all communities highlighted poor road
        infrastructure as a major barrier to mobility, trade, and accessing essential services. In Birlik and
        Kotormo, the roads are frequently damaged, making it difficult to transport goods to markets.
        Women in Noot Chaar-Tash and Toluk Almaluu pointed out that seasonal floods cut off villages,
        leaving families isolated.


11
     SMEC (draft ESIA) 2025
                                                                                                                      15

                                                                                                              Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework
      - Participation in Meetings: Village meetings are held only upon receiving notifications from
        higher authorities. The main topics discussed typically include animal migration, nomadism,
        road conditions, social infrastructure (such as kindergartens and hospitals), and irrigation
        canals. Women in Chaar-Tash Village revealed that they rarely participate in these meetings.
        However, this is not due to any explicit restrictions but rather a general lack of interest, as
        discussions often revolve around property matters that many find unengaging.
4.4.5 Education
60.     According to the Socio-economic Passport of Toguz-Toro District, in 2024, a total of 547
students are enrolled in general education institutions and 135 students enrolled in initial
professional education. Generally, the majority of students are enrolled in general education
institutions, while significantly fewer students pursue professional education. In addition, the number
of graduates from professional education programs is relatively low. A large proportion of student
complete 9th grade, but fewer continue to 11th grade or higher education.
61.    In the 2023/2024 academic year, the project AAs are home to 15 daytime primary and
secondary schools, accommodating 5,693 students at the primary and secondary high school levels.
Total number of teachers are 514, and number of technical staff are 174.
62.    The KIIs conducted with principals of secondary schools across affected communities and
FGDs with the local communities, including women groups during the socio-economic survey in 2024
revealed key education issues in affected AAs as following:
      - Overcrowding and poor school infrastructure: Across multiple villages, school overcrowding
        emerged as a critical issue. In communities such as Nichke-Sai, Kamysh-Bashy, Toluk, and
        Almaluu, student populations exceed school capacities by 1.5 to 2.6 times, forcing many
        institutions to operate in double or even triple shifts. Additionally, schools in Buurkan, Kamysh-
        Bashy, and Toluk suffer from deteriorating infrastructure, with urgent needs for renovations.
        Unsafe drinking water and inadequate sanitation were also highlighted as major concerns,
        particularly in Aral and Buurkan, where schools rely on wells for drinking water. In some cases,
        such as Nichke-Sai, Kamysh-Bashy, and Toluk, the complete absence of proper water sources
        forces students to wash their hands in nearby rivers.
      - Lack of sanitation and hygiene facilities: School principals from Nichke-Sai, Kamysh-Bashy, and
        Buurkan expressed deep concerns about inadequate sanitation facilities. Many schools still rely
        on outdoor toilets without proper sewage systems, creating hygiene risks for students. Buurkan
        and Toluk schools, with only two washbasins for over 200 students, and Almaluu and Nichke-
        Sai schools, with just 4-6 washbasins for over 500 students, struggle to provide basic hygiene
        facilities. The lack of hot water access further exacerbates health risks, particularly for younger
        students and those with disabilities.
      - Shortage of teachers: A severe shortage of qualified teachers, particularly for science subjects
        like chemistry and physics, was reported in Toluk, Kamysh-Bashy, and Buurkan. Many schools
        either lack these teachers altogether or rely on biology instructors to teach unrelated subjects.
        Recruiting educators for rural schools remains challenging due to low salaries, poor working
        conditions, and high turnover rates. The lack of specialized teachers further diminishes the
        quality of education, limiting students' ability to compete in higher education & technical fields.
      - Limited access to kindergartens and early education: Women in multiple villages emphasized
        the urgent need for kindergartens, particularly in Nichke-Sai and Toluk Almaluu, where working
        mothers struggle to balance employment and childcare. In Kyzyl-Ozgorush and Kotormo, the
        lack of proper school facilities forces some children to walk several kilometers to attend school,
                                                                                                                 16

                                                                                                         Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program    Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework
          creating safety concerns for parents. Women also voiced fears that potential forced
          resettlements could further disrupt children's education, as new locations might lack adequate
          schooling infrastructure.
      - Transportation barriers to education: Poor road conditions and lack of public transportation
        further limit access to education, particularly in remote villages like Toluk, Noot, and Nichke-
        Sai. In Aral and Kotormo, the absence of bridges makes it difficult for students to reach schools,
        especially during floods. Meanwhile, officials in Birlik and Kyzyl-Ozgorush highlighted the lack
        of street lighting, which makes it unsafe for students, particularly girls, to walk to school in early
        morning or evening hours. In many rural areas, students rely on private transportation, which
        can be costly and inconsistent.
4.4.6 Health
63.    The KIIs conducted with Directors of Healthcare Institutions across various communities
during the socio-economic survey in 2024 revealed key healthcare issues in affected AAs as follows:
      •    Shortage of Medical Personnel and Resources: Across all AAs, healthcare institutions are
           severely understaffed, with most facilities having only one or two doctors or, in some cases,
           none at all. In most villages, nurses and paramedics are the primary healthcare providers,
           often performing multiple roles due to the lack of specialized professionals. This is particularly
           evident in remote villages like Buurkan, Chorgochu, and Birdik, where residents must travel
           long distances for medical consultations. The absence of specialists forces patients to seek
           treatment in urban centers, creating additional financial and logistical burdens. Migration of
           medical professionals to cities, along with low rural salaries, has further exacerbated the
           shortage. Additionally, many healthcare facilities lack basic medical equipment, including X-
           ray machines, ultrasound scanners, and laboratory testing tools, making accurate diagnosis
           and treatment nearly impossible.
      •    Infrastructure deficiencies: The physical condition of healthcare facilities in all AAs is in dire
           need of improvement. Most buildings were constructed decades ago and have deteriorated
           over time. Reports from Atai, Nichke-Sai, and Chorgochu highlight significant structural issues,
           such as leaking roofs, poor insulation, broken windows, and inadequate heating systems,
           making healthcare delivery challenging, especially during winter. In rural health posts located
           in Buurkan and Chorgochu, the situation is even worse, as these facilities operate without
           functional water supply or sewage systems, forcing staff to manually fetch water for daily
           medical operations. In Nichke-Sai, Chorgochu, and Buurkan, the lack of running water means
           medical workers must rely on springs or irrigation ditches, raising serious hygiene concerns.
           The use of outdated computers that frequently freeze also hampers the transition to digital
           medical records, affecting the efficiency of patient care.
      •    Difficult access to healthcare: Transportation challenges pose a significant barrier to
           healthcare access, particularly in remote villages. In Buurkan, Chorgochu, and Birdik, the
           nearest district hospital is over 50 km away, making emergency referrals difficult. Poor road
           conditions, especially during winter, delay patient transport to medical facilities, often leading
           to severe complications or even fatalities. Pregnant women and critically ill patients in villages
           like Atai and Kyzyl-Ozgorush struggle to reach hospitals in time, leading to an increase in home
           births and preventable medical complications. The lack of public transportation further
           worsens the situation, forcing residents to either walk long distances or rely on expensive
           private transport to access healthcare services.


                                                                                                                  17

                                                                                                          Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program     Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework
      •   Economic hardship hinder healthcare access: The correlation between economic hardship
          and poor health is evident across all affected AAs. Many residents struggle to afford proper
          nutrition or preventive healthcare services. In facilities that charge consultation and
          treatment fees, many villagers postpone seeking medical attention due to financial
          constraints, often resulting in late-stage diagnoses and higher mortality rates. This is
          particularly severe in Atai and Kyzyl-Ozgorush, where many families live below the poverty
          line, further restricting their access to necessary medical care.
4.4.7 Land use
64.     As indicated above, the land use in the project area is characterized by few settlements,
barren public land and by the predominance of pastures. The present land use around the proposed
K1 HPP is mostly covered by pasture or meadows, bushlands, exposed rock, open river islands in the
valley floor and the foothill areas, while few coniferous trees along with shrubland, and fractured
exposed land are found in the highland and steep slope zones (See Table 6 and Figure 7).

rainfed land arable land make up 1.1% and 0.9%, respectively.

•        Pastures
66.     The pastures are located mainly in the flood plain of the Naryn river and on the gentle slopes
of the valleys. The pasture lands fall into three categories depending on their seasonal use: i) summer
pastures from 2,500 to 3,500 meters above sea level (m.a.s.l.); ii) spring-autumn pastures from 1,500
to 2,500 m.a.s.l., and iii) winter pastures, often near settlements located below 1,500 m.a.s.l.. Winter
pastures are the most valuable pastures for local people as they are critical for feeding animals during
the winter season. As indicated in the Table below, the largest share consists of summer pastures,
approximately 56% of the total area, followed by spring-autumn pastures at 32%, and winter
pastures, which account for just over 10%.

71.     The survey also reveals that pasture usage comes at a cost for villagers, with the majority
(70%) paying between 1,000–5,000 soms per year. A smaller 10% pay between 5,000–10,000 soms,
while only 4% incur higher costs ranging from 10,001–20,000 soms. Notably, 7% of respondents were
uncertain about their pasture-related payments, possibly indicating a lack of awareness or variations
in local payment systems. The data suggests that while most households bear a moderate financial
burden, a small percentage faces significantly higher pasture fees, which could be linked to factors
such as livestock numbers, pasture quality, or land ownership arrangements.
4.4.8 Settlements patterns
72.     As indicated above, around 13,000 people live permanently around the project area in
scattered settlements and three main rural centers : Nichke-Sai, Toluk and Sary-Kamysh (see Figure
10). These settlements are above the gorge of the Naryn river. They have all the basic social
infrastructures (i.e. primary school, health center, shops etc.). No permanent settlement exists in the
inundated area or in the SPZ.
73.     According to the ESIA, the settlement patterns in the project area are a blend of traditional
Kyrgyz lifestyles and adaptations to the region's natural environment. The villages are small,
agriculturally oriented, and closely tied to the Naryn River and its tributaries. It reflects the rural and
pastoral nature of the region, shaped by the area's topography and natural resources.
74.     The settlements are dispersed villages that align with the river valleys and accessible flatlands.
The clustered settlements are often located within a few kilometres of each other, forming pockets
of habitation amidst vast open spaces.
75.     While arable land is limited, settlements are strategically placed near patches of irrigable land.
These irrigated fields, located within 2-10 kilometres of villages, support small-scale agriculture,
primarily for household consumption and livestock fodder. Villages near the inundated area reflect
this pattern, with settlements adjacent to areas suitable for cultivating crops such as corn, potatoes,
hay, and seasonal vegetables.
76.      The housing in these settlements is modest. Houses are often single-story structures built with
locally available materials such as stone and wood. In addition to permanent homes, yurts (traditional
nomadic dwellings) are commonly used, especially during the summer grazing season

assessed in detail during the census, Inventory of Losses (IOL) and socioeconomic surveys to be
conducted during LRRPs’ preparation.
•        Pastures
81.     According to an IFAD report14, following independence from the Union of Soviet Socialist
Republics (USSR) in 1991, Kyrgyzstan, with over half of its land classified as pasture, launched rural
reforms primarily aimed at land privatization and the establishment of market based farming.
Foremost among these reforms was the dissolution of collective and state farms, and their
privatization. As collective and state farms were dissolved during the transition to a market-oriented
economy, their respective responsibilities were either terminated or transferred to other existing or
newly formed state structures requiring the involvement of the rural communities.
82.    In 2009, the Law on Pastures of the Kyrgyz Republic was passed, categorizing all pastureland
as a national treasure, never to be shifted to private ownership. All rights and authority to manage
pastures were legally moved to the National Public Pasture Users’ Association and local pasture
committees and community organizations of herders.
83.    In addition to the Law on Pastures, pastures in Kyrgyzstan are also managed in accordance
with the Presidential Decree “On the Implementation of the Administrative-Territorial Reform in Pilot
Mode at the Level of Aiyl Aimaks and Cities of the Kyrgyz Republic” dated December 29, 2023, No.
UP-37015. According to the Presidential Decree, local pastures are managed by Municipal Enterprises.
84.    This same resolution also includes procedure for determining fees for the use of pastures,
according to which Municipal Enterprises currently calculate grazing fees per livestock unit. In pasture
management, the term "livestock unit" (LU) is used. The LU is a reference unit that facilitates the
aggregation of livestock from various species and age groups using specific coefficients, initially based
on the nutritional or feed requirements of each animal type. Even if Municipal Enterprises might lose



14 IFAD (2022), Introduction to community pasture management plans in Kyrgyzstan, Synthesis of the country’s rural

pastoral development and lessons learned through IFAD-funded projects

Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program     Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework
part of their revenue due to the 1 HPP project, they are expected to have less pastureland to manage
and they may get additional revenue for allocation of existing pastures to the affected herders.
85.    These Municipal Enterprises annually grant rights for pasture use based on the Pasture Use
and Management Plan. The document confirming the right to use pasture is the Pasture Use Ticket;
a standard form of this ticket is provided in the Resolution of the Government of the Kyrgyz Republic
“On Measures for the Implementation of the Law of the Kyrgyz Republic ‘On Pastures’” dated June
19, 2009, No. 386.
86.       Municipal Enterprises have the following responsibilities:
      -   Development and implementation of a community plan and an annual plan for pasture
          management and use;
      -   Monitoring the condition of pastures;
      -   Issuing pasture use tickets in accordance with the annual pasture use plan, as well as
          concluding agreements for the use of pastures for other purposes;
      -   Determining the amount of fees for pasture use and ensuring their mandatory approval by
          the local council;
      -   Resolving disputes related to pasture use within the scope of its authority;
      -   Managing revenues collected from pasture and other resource use fees that are directed
          toward investments in pasture infrastructure and allocated, in accordance with the legislation
          of the Kyrgyz Republic, for the maintenance, management, and improvement of pastures.
87.       .
88.     During field visits it was confirmed that no pasture users in the project area have land
ownership documents. Some of the pasture users requested the land ownership title but were
rejected. However, most of them reported having updated pasture registration papers (pasture
ticket) and paying taxes.

92.    Pastures Management Plans. Municipal Entreprises have to prepare pastures management
plans. There are two types of pasture plans. The first is the Pasture Management Plan, which is
developed once every five years and serves as a short-term strategic planning document. The second
is the Annual Pasture Use Plan, developed every year and used as the basis for budgeting its
implementation. Both plans are developed according to the procedure established by the Order of
the Ministry of Water Resources, Agriculture, and Processing Industry of the Kyrgyz Republic under
the Model Regulation on the Procedure for Developing the Pasture Management Plan and the Annual
Pasture Use Plan, dated May 15, 2024, No. 04-13/152.
93.     The Pasture Use Plan includes a grazing schedule, which assigns usage periods for each
pasture area and calculates the livestock stocking rate for every pasture plot. The stocking rate is
calculated to prevent damage to the botanical composition and productivity of the pasture
vegetation. This rate is determined according to the Regulation on the Procedure for Determining
Livestock Stocking Rates on Pasture Lands of the Kyrgyz Republic dated February 10, 2021, No. 36.

allocated to the company, are located within the inundation zone. The company exploits timber
& non-timber forest products. The main types of trees are birch and ash.

4.4.10 Livelihoods
97.     The project area has a local economy and level of livelihoods that are strongly tied to livestock
breeding, as well as seasonal and traditional ways of migrating. Agriculture also plays a significant
role, with local communities engaged in livestock rearing, crop farming, beekeeping and the
production of dairy products. The primary sources of livelihood in the affected villages are rooted in
traditional economic practices. Livestock herding remains the main livelihood.
•     Livestock herding
98.     The project area’s economy and lifestyle are heavily influenced by pastoralism. For
generations, shepherd families have moved with their herds from the surrounding villages to the
natural pastures located in the project area, where they stay for several months. This is the main
livelihood and source of income for most of these communities. In Toluk, the livelihoods of the entire
village depend on the winter pastures located in the project area. The loss of pastures will therefore
have direct impacts on the herders’ income.
99.    As of the beginning of 2025, the total livestock population across the aiyl aimaks was 31,782
corresponding to 12,890.8 Livestock Units (LU), according to data provided by the Aiyl Aimaks (see
Table 9 and Figure 12). The most commonly herded animals include: i) Sheep and goats (74%): used
for meat, wool, and dairy production; Cattle (cows and bulls) (16%): raised for milk, meat, and trade;
and Horses (10%): mainly for transportation and traditional farming activities

100.    According to an IFAD document16, pastoral systems, if well managed, are the best suited and
adaptive form of agriculture for the majority of Kyrgyzstan’s land area, that is too dry, cold, or
mountainous to practise crop farming. The production system relies on livestock mobility as a key
strategy that allows herders to mitigate risks and manage pasture and water resources efficiently.
Livestock are also important for rural household food security, provision of regular income and acting
as a social safety net because they can be sold in times of hardship and economic distress.
