             Milk (t) per 1000 ha                                 0.45      10.51           4.74
             Meat (t) per 1000 people                            39.81      20.11          29.87
             Milk (t) per 1000 people                             2.26      54.92          63.64
           Source: Pasture Management Report, 2025
102.    The table shows that in Imanaliev Ayil Aimak, the highest amount of meat is produced per
1,000 people, followed by Sary-Kamysh. Interestingly, in both AAs, the share of small ruminants in
the total livestock population is higher. Sheep are generally kept for a single purpose, meat, whereas
cattle are also kept for milk production. This is confirmed by the data from Toluk, where there are
691.18 heads of cattle per 1,000 people, and 10.51 tons of milk are produced per 1,000 people, the
second-highest figure among the three AAs, after Sary-Kamysh, which also has a higher number of
cattle per 1,000 people than the others.
103.    None of the AAs provide data on wool production. In practice, wool has brought little to no
income in recent years due to its low market value. Moreover, the wool currently produced is coarse
rather than fine, and is therefore not accepted by buyers.
104.   Based on this data, it is difficult to assess the efficiency of pasture management. For example,
Imanaliev and Toluk AAs have nearly the same pasture area per 1,000 people, while Sary-Kamysh has
2.5 times more pasture per capita than the other two, yet its production levels remain lower.
105.    It has been found that growth in livestock production has also made a significant contribution
to the reduction of rural poverty. By increasing both incomes and wealth, livestock production has
improved the welfare and reduced the vulnerability of people. However, as reported during KIIs and
FGDs, there are several problems affecting the sector, which includes the following:
    - Water scarcity is a major issue, with many villages lacking clean drinking water for both people
        and livestock. In Birlik and Kotormo, villagers walk 600 meters to fetch water, while others
        rely on contaminated sources. In Aral and Noot villages, some farmers drill shallow wells, but
        water quality is poor, leading to disease outbreaks among livestock. Without proper irrigation,
        sustaining herds remains difficult.
    - Another major issue is pasture degradation and overgrazing. As livestock farming is the
        primary source of income in these villages, pastures are often overused, leading to soil
        depletion, erosion, and declining grass quality. Farmers in Toluk and Kyzyl-Ozgorush report
        that grasslands are shrinking, making it harder to feed livestock. In Nichke-Sai, the
        deteriorating condition of pastures has forced farmers to reduce their herd sizes, as there is
        not enough grazing land to sustain large numbers of animals.
    - Poor infrastructure and market access hinder economic growth. Many villages are isolated,
        with poor road networks making it difficult for farmers to transport their livestock to markets
        or access essential supplies. In Toluk and Noat, bad road conditions prevent farmers from
        selling their livestock products efficiently, limiting their income potential.


                                                                                                                     28

                                                                                                             Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework
       -   Finally, climate change is increasingly affecting livestock herding. Farmers in Toluk and Kyzyl-
           Ozgorush have observed a decline in rainfall, drier soil conditions, and colder winters, all of
           which reduce pasture productivity and make livestock management more challenging.
106.   From the governance and administration aspects, there are also several problems affecting
the sector as follows:
    - Reduction in livestock and change in ownership patterns: Following the dissolution of the
       collective and state farms and the distribution of their livestock to the former workers,
       livestock numbers declined rapidly after Independence (1991), as large numbers of animals
       were sold or slaughtered. This resulted in abnormal age structures of the national herd and
       grossly distorted succession ratios, slowing the subsequent expansion of the industry.
    107.        Shift from public to private livestock ownership: Presently, smallholder production
       systems predominate, and among smallholders, individual farmers have steadily replaced
       household plots as the main source of output and sales. While household plots have continued
       their active involvement in livestock production, as before Independence, their limited access
       to land and capital has constrained their ability to expand output as far and as fast as private
       farmers. The private farming sector has driven the growth in livestock sector output, while
       livestock output in the household subsector has stagnated.
    - Large skill gap: Before Independence, only collective and state farm workers had skills in
       livestock rearing. After privatization, the manpower engaged in livestock did not have the
       requisite skills of using improved technology. Thus, the livestock productivity has not
       improved since 1996.
    108.        Poor pasture management: The basis for effective and sustainable pasture use that
       had characterized the pre-Soviet transhumant pattern largely disintegrated after
       independence. The institutional arrangements that had governed the adaptation of these
       arrangements to the collective farming system also discontinued. With this background,
       present day pasture-based animal raising in Kyrgyzstan has been pushed into an annual cycle
       of insufficient pasture production and overgrazing, which is unproductive, unsustainable and
       self-perpetuating. The small farmers who now own most of the animals tend to graze them
       on near-by pastures as much as possible, which results in overuse of these pastures. This is
       also leading to conflict between families as well as between villages17.
•      Beekeeping
109.    Beekeeping is a small yet significant livelihood activity in the project area, practiced by a
limited number of households. According to the FGDs results, the main findings regarding beekeeping
are summarized below:
    - Sary-Kamysh AA (Birlik and Kotormo villages): Around 5% of households engage in
        beekeeping, which equates to approximately 20 households.
    110.        Atai AA (Aral village): Beekeeping existsbut remains a secondary activity to livestock
        farming.
    - E. Imanaliev AA (Kyzyl-Ozgorush and Nichke-Sai villages): In Kyzyl-Ozgorush, beekeeping
        involves about 5% of the population (roughly 50 people). In Nichke-Sai, the practice is much
        rarer, with only three identified beekeepers.
    111.        Toluk AA (Toluk, Noot, and Chaar-Tash villages): Toluk village has the highest
        concentration of beekeepers, with 20% of residents engaged in the activity, making it a more



17
     Op. Cite Footnote 3
                                                                                                                 29

                                                                                                         Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework
         significant livelihood. In Noot and Chaar-Tash villages, beekeeping contributes to 10-20% of
         local economic activities, indicating moderate participation.
112.     Despite its presence, beekeeping is generally not a primary source of income but serves as a
supplementary livelihood alongside livestock farming. Most villagers rely on cattle breeding, while
beekeeping provides seasonal income, primarily from honey production. In Nichke-Sai, residents
mainly sell dairy and meat products, with beekeeping remaining a secondary trade. Meanwhile, in
Toluk, beekeeping contributes to 30% of household incomes, alongside land use and other small-scale
activities.
113.    Beekeepers in these regions face several challenges. The lack of irrigation and water access
significantly impacts honey production by limiting the growth of flowering plants essential for bees.
Poor road infrastructure also makes it difficult for beekeepers to access pastures or fields rich in wild
plants, further restricting honey production. In some villages, beekeepers depend on natural wild
plant ecosystems, which are at risk due to erosion, drought, and environmental degradation.
114.   Additionally, FGD participants expressed concerns that the construction of the K1-HPP could
threaten beekeeping by causing vegetation loss, reducing the availability of wildflowers and herbs
necessary for bee colonies to thrive.
115.     Fishing
116.    Fishing activities are concentrated in the Toktogul reservoir (out of the K1 impact area). Some
villagers may fish in the Naryn river but for their own consumption and small scale. Small-scale fish
breeding has also been noticed in Atai AA.
•        Agriculture
117.  Crop farming in the surveyed villages is limited and primarily practiced for personal
consumption rather than large-scale commercial production. However, some villages cultivate
vegetables, grains, and fruit crops, often as a supplementary livelihood to livestock farming.
118.   Several key crops are cultivated, primarily for household consumption and livestock feed. The
main crops grown include vegetables, grains, and fruit crops.
    ▪    Vegetables:
    -    Potatoes: One of the most widely grown crops, particularly in Aral, Kyzyl-Ozgorush, and Toluk
         villages. Potatoes are an important staple for local households and are sometimes sold in
         nearby markets.
    -    Cabbage and tomatoes: These crops are cultivated mainly in Toluk village, where residents
         have better access to irrigation from highland canals. These vegetables are used for household
         consumption and occasionally for small-scale trade.
    -    Greens and medicinal herbs: Some villages, such as Birlik and Kotormo, grow small amounts
         of greens and medicinal plants, though this is not a major economic activity.
    ▪    Grains and fodder crops:
    -    Wheat: Grown in Toluk village, though on a small scale due to limited water access.
    -    Grass and hay: Farmers in Kyzyl-Ozgorush village and Aral village grow grass and fodder crops
         for livestock feed, as animal husbandry remains the primary livelihood.
    ▪    Fruit Crops
    -    Apples and cherries: These fruits are cultivated in Toluk village in household gardens.
    -    Walnuts and Currants: Found in Kyzyl-Ozgorush village, but production is limited due to water
         shortages.

                                                                                                                 30

                                                                                                         Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework
119.   Farmers across these villages face several challenges in crop farming:
    -  The most significant is water scarcity and inadequate irrigation systems. Many villages rely on
       unreliable water sources, often collecting water manually from distant wells or rivers. Farmers
       in Birlik and Kotormo believe that if irrigation were improved, they could expand into tree and
       vegetable farming. In Aral and Noat, some residents have dug shallow wells, but these often
       fail to meet clean water standards. In Kyzyl-Ozgorush, 40% of cultivated land remains non-
       irrigated, limiting crop yields.
    - Poor road infrastructure is another major barrier to crop farming. Without proper roads,
       farmers struggle to transport crops to markets. In Toluk, bad roads have led to the closure of
       small-scale agricultural businesses, further limiting income opportunities.
    - Climate and environmental concerns pose long-term risks to agriculture. In Toluk and Kyzyl-
       Ozgorush, grass and plant growth have already declined due to environmental changes,
       raising concerns about future agricultural viability. Participants in the FGDs reported that
       precipitation has become more irregular, leading to longer dry spells that negatively impact
       crop yields and pasture conditions.
    120.         The lack of access to agricultural inputs and markets further restricts crop farming.
       Villages normally lack local processing facilities, preventing farmers from adding value to their
       crops. In Kyzyl-Ozgorush, farmers struggle to store or process produce, making it difficult to
       sell at good prices. Without investment in irrigation, roads, and market access, crop farming
       remains a limited and unreliable source of income Beekeepers are present in the project area,
       as it is usual in the Kyrgyz Republic.

4.4.11 Road System
121.   FGDs with local communities including local authorities, farmers and women groups during
the SES identified several issues relating to road infrastructure in the affected AAs as follows:
    - Across the affected AAs, poor road infrastructure was identified as a major barrier to mobility,
       trade, and access to essential services. Local authorities from Toluk, Noot, and Nichke-Sai
       raised concerns about the deteriorating condition of roads leading to pastures and markets,
       making transportation of agricultural goods difficult. In Sary-Kamysh AA, officials specifically
       noted that frequent flooding of the Küköy-Meren River disrupts access to grazing areas and
       market centers, further complicating economic activities for farmers and herders.
    - Women across different villages also highlighted the impact of poor roads on daily life. In Birlik
       and Kotormo, women emphasized that damaged roads create significant challenges in
       transporting goods to markets, leading to financial losses for small-scale traders. Women in
       Noot Chaar-Tash and Toluk Almaluu reported that seasonal floods often cut off villages,
       leaving families isolated and unable to reach healthcare centers, schools, or marketplaces.
    - In addition to poor road conditions, the absence of bridges was flagged as a significant issue
       in Aral and Kotormo villages. Authorities in these areas noted that the lack of bridges makes
       it difficult for residents to access schools, hospitals, and markets, especially during floods. The
       situation is particularly dire during the rainy season, when river crossings become impassable,
       further isolating communities.
    - Limited or non-existent public transportation was another major concern raised by both local
       authorities and residents. Officials in Toluk and Aral pointed out that many villages lack any
       form of public transit, forcing residents to rely on private vehicles or expensive alternative
       transport options. This issue disproportionately affects students and elderly residents, who
       often struggle to reach schools and medical facilities. In Birlik and Kotormo villages, women


                                                                                                                 31

                                                                                                         Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework
         noted that the absence of reliable transportation options makes it difficult for them to engage
         in economic activities outside their immediate communities.
    -    In some villages, poor road infrastructure is compounded by the lack of street lighting, making
         night time travel unsafe. Authorities in Birlik and Kyzyl-Ozgorush raised concerns that the
         absence of proper lighting increases risks for pedestrians and drivers alike. This issue was
         particularly emphasized by women, who expressed fears about traveling at night due to
         security concerns.
4.4.12 Water Supply
122.   Water shortages are a major concern across all affected AAs, affecting both drinking water
access and irrigation for agriculture. Local authorities, farmers, and women’s groups have raised
serious concerns about the poor quality and insufficient availability of water. Many areas rely on
untreated sources, leading to significant health risks and agricultural difficulties.
123.    Drinking water shortage In Atai and E. Imanaliev AAs, many households depend on wells for
drinking water, but poor water quality and the lack of filtration systems make it unsafe for
consumption. In Kyzyl-Ozgorush village, residents mostly rely on untreated river water, which has led
to frequent outbreaks of waterborne diseases such as hepatitis and gastrointestinal infections.
Similarly, in Toluk and Imanaliev villages, access to clean drinking water remains a critical issue. In
Birlik and Kotormo villages, villagers rely on open water sources without proper filtration, further
increasing health risks for the population.
124.    Women in multiple communities also voiced their concerns over water shortages for both
household and agricultural use. In Noot Chaar-Tash and Kyzyl-Ozgorush villages, women reported
that their families frequently suffer from waterborne diseases, particularly affecting children, due to
the reliance on untreated river water. In Toluk Almaluu village, the existing water pipelines are old
and dysfunctional. Some women fear that the construction of a hydropower plant (HPP) will further
reduce their already limited access to clean drinking water.
125.    Irrigation shortages. Irrigation shortages are another major issue affecting agriculture.
Farmers in Noot and Chaar-Tash villages struggle with outdated or damaged irrigation canals, which
makes it difficult to maintain crop production. The lack of reliable irrigation infrastructure in Kyzyl-
Ozgorush village has worsened agricultural difficulties, as farmers face challenges in sustaining their
fields due to inconsistent water supply.
126.    Across all Focus Group Discussions (FGDs), women, farmers, and local authorities strongly
demanded urgent action to address the water crisis. They called for new water supply systems with
proper filtration, rehabilitation of irrigation infrastructure to improve agricultural productivity, and
inclusion in national water projects such as the Taza-Suu Project.
4.4.13 Issues with Past Land Acquisition
127.   Communities have mentioned during consultations that there were some legacy issues
(delayed or inadequate compensation) associated with past land acquisition and related to the 1992
earthquake and the K2-HPP construction.
•        1992 Earthquake
128.    During site visits and consultations, some citizens reported that on 19 August 1992, there was
a big earthquake in the area. At that time, the Prime Minister told the local communities (i.e. Toluk
and Samy-Karmysh AAs) that they will have to relocate due to the earthquake risks in the area. The
government issued a decree (Decree #485, September 29, 1992) allocating land (17 ha) in the village
of Ak-Torpok (Chui region, around 50 km West of Bishkek and 200-220 km from Toluk/Sary-Kamysh)
                                                                                                                 32

                                                                                                         Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework
and in the Village of Jany-Zher (Bakter region, South-West of the project area around 500 km from
Toluk/Sary-Kamysh) for the citizens located in the areas subject to earthquake. However, at this time,
many residents did not want to move and stayed in the project area. Some however have already
been resettled in the village of Ak-Torpok. It was reported by local authorities that this Decree is still
in force.
129.     Local authorities mentioned that, due to the K1-HPP project and the loss of pastures, the
government could consider allocating these lands to the residents of Toluk/Sary-Kamysh in case of
flooding of their pastures. During public consultation, citizens also indicated that if, at the time, the
residents of Toluk/Sary-Kamysh did not want relocate, they now consider that, if the Government still
accept to allocate land to them in the village of Ak-Torpok, they may accept and could restore their
livelihood at this new location.
•        K2-HPP
130.   Kambarata-2 hydroelectric plant (K2-HPP) is located 14.5 km downstream of the K1-HPP.
Construction of the K2-HPP was launched in 1986 but halted in 1991 with the collapse of the former
Soviet Union. Construction work resumed in 2007 with the commissioning of the first of three
planned 120 MW units in November 2010. The K2-HPP was funded partly by Russia. Land acquisition
was necessary for the construction of the dam and for the reservoir area
131.   K2-HPP is located only 14.5 km downstream of K1-HPP and therefore affected the same
communities. During consultation and site visits in Nichke-Say and-Uch Terek (E. Imanaliev AA), local
authorities indicated that land compensation was not fully implemented for the K2-HPP (i.e. delayed
or inadequate payments).
•        Proposed actions
132.    Both the land acquisition issues for the 1992 earthquake and for the K2-HPP are not directly
related to the K1-HPP but it can create distrust and resistance among the local population for the K1-
HPP as it affects the same communities. The following actions should be taken to provide answers to
the citizens.
133.    Assessment of the situation for the K2-HPP. Additional information from local communities
and people affected by the K2-HPP is necessary to better understand the compensation packages
offered to affected households and what was committed and not implemented. As part of the
preparation of the LRRP1 (construction site, early works, and associated facilities) a due diligence
should be conducted on the K2-HPP land acquisition process to assess: i) types of compensation
packages offered/received; ii) compliance with Kyrgyz regulations; iii) outstanding issues (i.e.
complaints, compensation not received etc.); and iv) lessons learned from the K2-HPP experience.
Based on this assessment, correctives actions, if needed, will be proposed. Assessment of the
situation will be done through consultation with national agencies (i.e. K2-HPP operating company,
Ministry of Emergency Situations, local authorities)) to check on their knowledge of the situation and
any measures having been taken or planned to be taken and with local authorities and affected
households through public meetings and FGDs.
134.    Land to be allocated in Ak-Torpok village or in other villages. It is suggested that during the
update of the LRRP (see Chapter 6), at the time of LRRP2 preparation, through consultation with local
authorities/communities, the feasibility and sustainability to relocate interested PAPs in Ak-Torpok
or in other villages, based on Decree #485, for PAPs losing pastures in Toluk and Sary-Kamysh will be
considered. This would allow younger generations, who currently live in areas with limited economic
opportunities, to build better lives closer to urban centers, where there is more room for self-

                                                                                                                 33

                                                                                                         Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework
realization and employment. However, state-level support is essential to reach a positive resolution
and enable the community to access the land that was once designated for them.
135.    Meaningful consultation. Delayed or inadequate compensation from previous projects may
led to distrust and resistance for the K1-HPP. Therefore, to prevent such conflicts for the K1-HPP, it is
essential to conduct early and transparent consultations with stakeholders, ensure that affected
people are involved in resettlement planning, their concerns will be taken into consideration into the
LRRPs preparation. It will help to restore confidence of the citizens and will avoid that people may
feel excluded from decision-making, further exacerbating tensions.

5 LEGAL REVIEW
136.   This LRRF has been prepared in compliance with the applicable and relevant laws of the Kyrgyz
Republic related to land acquisition, resettlement support, and livelihood restoration, and in
compliance with the World Bank’s Environmental and Social Framework (ESF) in general and
Environmental and Social Standard 5 (ESS5) on Land Acquisition, Restrictions on Land Use and
Involuntary Resettlement in particular.
137.    This section of the LRRF provides an overview of the policies/legal framework and procedures
for land acquisition and resettlement assessment in the Kyrgyz Republic, as well as relevant World
Bank environmental and social standards applicable to project activities. Each area of activity selected
within the project will be checked, classified and evaluated according to the Environmental and Social
Management Framework and the legislation of the Kyrgyz Republic, and is to be reviewed and
approved by the WB as appropriate.
138.    The project's legal and policy principles are based on national laws and regulations on land
acquisition and compensation policy in the Kyrgyz Republic and ESS5 Land Acquisition, Restrictions
on Land Use and Involuntary Resettlement.
5.1 Applicable National and State Laws Policies
139.   Constitution of the Kyrgyz Republic, May 5, 2021 - Pursuant to the Constitution, land may be
under private, state, and municipal ownership. The Constitution recognizes the diversity of forms of
ownership, and guarantees equal legal protection of private, state, municipal and other forms of
ownership (Article 12, para 1). Property is inviolable. No one may be arbitrarily deprived of his
property. Seizure of property by the state against the will of the owner is allowed only by a court
decision Article 12, para 2). Seizure of property for public needs, as defined in the Basic Law, may be
carried out by a court decision with fair and prior provision for compensation for the value of this
property and other losses caused as a result of the alienation (Article 15, para 2.). Land, with the
exception of pastures and forests, may be in private and municipal forms of ownership Article 16,
para 3).
140.     Land Code of the KR dated July 18, 2025 , No. 149 -The Land Code is the major legal
instrument regulating land acquisition for state and public needs. Pursuant to the Land Code, land
acquisition for state and public needs is one of the grounds for termination of title and use right to a
land plot. It defines state and public needs as needs related to national security, environment
protection and protection of historical and cultural heritage objects, placement, and maintenance of
social, industrial, transport, energy, engineering and construction facilities, development of mineral
deposits and implementation of international treaties.
141.   Land acquisition for public and state needs (Article 69) is permitted only in the absence of
alternative options for the placement of facilities and is carried out based on a decision of the
authorized body through a negotiated purchase agreement or, if no agreement is reached, by a court
decision. Compensation must be paid prior to the actual acquisition and includes the market value of
the land rights, buildings and structures located on the land, as well as all associated losses, with the
option to provide an equivalent land plot at the choice of the land rights holder.
142.    In terms of compensation payment (Article 111) the normative (cadastral) value of a land plot
is used to assess the amount of losses and damages caused. The land disputes arising from allocation,
expropriation and termination of land rights are resolved only by a court of law (Article 119).



                                                                                                                 35

                                                                                                         Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program    Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework
143.    Civil Code of the KR, Parts I and II (May 8, 1996, No. 15 and January 5, 1998, No. 1) - The Civil
Code defines the legal status of participants in land transactions, regulates contractual and other
obligations where land is the object. It applies to the relations arising in connection with land if such
relations are not regulated by the land legislation (Article 1).
144.    Provisions of the Civil Code regulating the land acquisition virtually duplicate provisions of the
Land Code. The land acquisition for public needs requires a decision of an authorized body (Article
233-18). The Civil Code sets forth requirements on the form and procedure for executing land
acquisition related transactions (purchase and sale, donation agreement, etc.) It also regulates the
procedure and conditions for establishing an easement and is consistent with the provisions of the
Land Code. The losses caused to an owner of immovable property by the establishment of a
compulsory easement are subject to compensation by the person in whose interests the easement
has been established. The owner impacted by a compulsory easement has the right to demand a
commensurate payment instead of the right to demand compensation for losses (Article 233-11).
145.     Law on State Registration of Rights of Immovable Properties and Associated Transactions
dated December 22, 1998, No.153 - This law states that state registration of rights of immovable
properties and associated transactions is a legal act of recognition and confirmation of rights to
immovable properties and their encumbrances (restrictions), providing protection in exercise for
those rights and restrictions, except as provided in this Law. These rights and restrictions, subject to
mandatory registration, include: (i) right to ownership; (ii) right to economic management; (iii) right
of operational management; (iv) right to unlimited use of the land plot; (v) rights arising from a
mortgage; (vi) right to temporary use, lease or sublease (for a period of three years or more); (vii)
easements (other than those specified in Article 6 of this Law); (viii) restrictions on rights to design,
build and use a separate unit of immovable property, except in accordance with other national
legislations; (ix) rights arising from court decisions; (x) environmental rights, the list of which is
established by national legislation; (xi) other rights, subject to registration in accordance with the Civil
Code and other regulatory acts; and (xii) rights arising from the legalization of property.
146.    Law of the KR “On Procedure for Consideration of Citizens’ Appeals” dated May 4, 2007, No.
67 - The Law on Citizens’ Appeals regulates relations arising in connection with the right of a person
to file appeals with state authorities and local self-government bodies as envisaged in the
Constitution. The said Law establishes the procedure for consideration of citizens’ appeals by state
authorities, local self-government bodies and officials. Citizens may make filings in written, electronic,
or verbal format in the form of proposals, applications, or complaints. Citizens may make filings,
including recommendations, request for help with exercising and restoring constitutional rights and
liberties of self or of other individuals, reporting violation of laws or other legal rights, as well as filing
information about deficiencies in the activities of the bodies and officials.
147.    Law of the KR “On Foundations of Administrative Activity and Administrative Procedure”
dated July 31, 2015, No. 210- This Law regulates the activity of state and municipal bodies for
interaction with individuals and legal entities. It sets forth requirements on administrative procedures
and documents, various procedural terms applicable to administrative bodies and applicants and
procedures for filing complaints against decisions and actions of administrative bodies. Complaints
filed by individuals and legal entities regarding actions of state and municipal bodies related to land
acquisition must be considered pursuant to the procedure established by this Law. Article 94.4 of the
Administrative Law distinguishes the scope of this Act with the law on appeals. The Law on Citizens’
Appeals does not cover the implementation of administrative procedures., while the Administrative
Law considers it, especially in the context of land acquisition.


                                                                                                                  36

                                                                                                          Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework
148.     Law of the KR “On Appraisal Activity” dated December 22, 2021, No. 155- This Law
establishes a legal basis and regulates relations arising in connection with appraisal activity in the KR.
It sets forth the requirement to carry out independent appraisal in case of forced expropriation of a
property for state or public needs, involving payment of compensation. In accordance with the Law,
an individual, certified appraiser or a legal entity having no less than two certified appraisers, may
engage in appraisal activities. Like the Land Code, the Law stipulates that cadastral value of a land
plot shall be used in calculation and payment of losses and damages caused by land acquisition for
state and public needs.
149.   Resolution of the Government of the KR Approving Property Appraisal Standards
Mandatory for all Subjects of Appraisal Activity (April 3, 2006, No. 217)- The Resolution makes
property appraisal standards binding for all subjects of appraisal activity in the KR based on basic
concepts and principles of appraisal, including business valuation, real estate (immovable property),
intangible assets and movable property.
150.    Law on Pastures of the Kyrgyz Republic (January 26, 2009, No. 30) This Law regulates pasture
management, improvement and use thereof. Article 3(1) of the Pasture Law specifies that all pastures
in Kyrgyzstan are publicly owned and their management is the responsibility of local communities.
The 2009 Law requested all pasture users to form pasture users’ unions (PUUs), which elect their own
executive bodies called Pastures Committees (PCs)18. These bodies are legally authorized to govern
the use of pastures, independent of state administrative control. PUUs have the following
competence: (a) elaboration of community pasture management plan; (b) elaboration of annual
pasture management plan; (c) implementation of both plans; (d) monitoring of the state of pastures;
(e) issuance of pasture authorization; (f) collection of fees for pasture use; (g) dispute settlement; and
(h) revenue management. Transfer of pastures in lease and sub-lease shall be prohibited. Herders are
entitled to use pasture land if they received an authorization and pay fees. Fees are determined by
each PUU, based on community and pasture land needs. Improvement of pasture infrastructure shall
be intended measures aimed at reconstruction, repair, rehabilitation, construction of pasture
infrastructure facilities. Approval of the boundaries of pastures is carried out by the Government of
the Kyrgyz Republic on the basis of the recommendations of the state commission for establishing
pasture boundaries. The use of pastures for other purposes is carried out on the basis of agreements
on the use of pastures for other purposes. Pasture use rights are to be allocated through pasture
tickets, which give the holder the right to a number of animal grazing days, as well as access to grazing
routes Intervention of state bodies and local state administration in the activities of local self-
government bodies and associations of pasture users on the use of pastures is not allowed, with the
exception of cases provided for by the legislation of the Kyrgyz Republic. Pasture users are responsible
to the association for the implementation of the community plan for the management and use of
pastures and the annual pasture use plan. At the request of a quarter of the pasture users included
in the association, any issue related to the competence of the executive body of pasture users
associations can be submitted to consideration of the general meeting of pasture users. The
community plan for the management and use of pastures is mandatory for all pasture users after its
approval by the representative body of local self-government.
151.    Regulation on public investments management (May 28, 2019, No. 232)- This regulation
establishes a unified procedure for initiation, selection, preparation, implementation, monitoring,
and evaluation of public investment projects, but does not apply to technical assistance projects, as

18 As per Presidential Decree “On the Implementation of the Administrative-Territorial Reform in
Pilot Mode at the Level of Aiyl Aimaks and Cities of the Kyrgyz Republic” dated December 29, 2023,
Municipal Enterprises replaced Pastures Committees.
                                                                                                 37

                                                                                                         Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework
well as grants not supported through the budget system. According to the Regulation, each
implementing agency is responsible for the timely completion of the necessary procedures related to
land allocation, and all land acquisition costs should be estimated and included in the project
implementation expenditures. The implementing agency should hold discussion with all interested
organizations and project beneficiaries during the preparation the project documentation and its
conclusions should be reflected in the documentation. The implementing agency is also obliged to
organize communication events whereby the beneficiaries and the public are informed about the
outcomes of the project implementation, as well as prepare data and information required for
evaluation of its social and economic impacts.
152.   Decree of the President of the KR on the Issues of Transfer of Land Plots Designated for the
Use of Renewable Sources of Energy (March 23, 2023, UP No. 62) - The Decree has been adopted to
expedite implementation of projects in the energy sector. Pursuant to the Decree, the Green Energy
Fund under the Cabinet of Ministers will be granted the termless right to use land plots suitable for
the renewable energy sources production. These land plots will be allocated from unoccupied state
& municipal land plots, as well as via land acquisition.
153.    Law of the Kyrgyz Republic “On the Fundamentals of Administrative Activities and
Administrative Procedures” (July 31, 2015, No. 210). The law regulates the actions of administrative
bodies in public law relations, including the adoption of acts and actions that have practical
consequences for individuals. According to the Article 62 an administrative act, action, or inaction of
an administrative body can be appealed in an administrative (pre-trial) procedure, and subsequently
in a judicial procedure.
154.    If a citizen disagrees with the decision/action of the Ministry of Energy, they can appeal to
the central body of the Ministry by submitting a complaint to the Ministry's leadership. If the citizen
is dissatisfied with the Ministry's decision, they have the right to go to court, if they believe their
rights have been violated and administrative measures were not taken in accordance with the law.
155.     According to Article 115 of the Administrative Procedure Code of the Kyrgyz Republic, the
court may return the claim if the plaintiff has not followed the legally established pre-trial dispute
resolution procedure, and the possibility of using this procedure has not been lost. This means that
before going to court, the citizen must go through all the prescribed stages of pre-trial resolution,
including appealing to the state authorities (in this case, the Ministry of Energy) and receiving an
official response.
156.    Law of the Kyrgyz Republic of February 22, 2024 No. 53 "On burial and funeral business".
The law regulates the actions regarding burial and funeral. Article 16 is on Reburial or exhumation of
the remains of the deceased. This article indicates that (Alinea 2), reburial of remains can be carried
out:
a. when making a decision to move the burial site in the event of a threat of constant flooding,
     landslides, earthquakes and other natural disasters;
b. at the request of investigative bodies and the prosecutor's office;
c. at the request of relatives of the deceased;
d. when previously unknown military burials are discovered;
e. when transporting remains from individual graves for reburial to other burial sites in the republic
     or outside of it.
157.   The reburial of remains is carried out by a specialized funeral organization on the basis of the
customer’s application, death certificate and the conclusion of local state sanitary supervision
authorities on the possibility of exhumation.

                                                                                                                 38

                                                                                                         Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework
158.    The remains are transferred from the graves in boxes with lids, the coffin is placed in the box
and transferred to a new location, and the grave is filled in. If the coffin in which the burial was carried
out is not preserved, its remains are burned in specially designated places in agreement with the state
sanitary supervision authorities. In the event of the need to move the burial site, the extraction of all
remains and their reburial at another burial site shall be carried out in compliance with the
requirements of the norms for exhumation and reburial specified in this article.
159.     Transport after transportation of remains is subject to disinfection. Owners are responsible
for the timeliness of disinfection of transport. The transfer of corpses and remains from cemeteries
is carried out under the supervision of state sanitary bodies. If it is necessary to move the burial site,
the extraction of all remains and their reburial at another burial site is carried out at the expense of
the organization using the territory of the former cemetery.
160.     Decree of the President of the Kyrgyz Republic dated October 14, 2024 UP No. 296 "On
measures to implement the Law of the Kyrgyz Republic "On burial and funeral business “In the
Kyrgyz Republic, every person has the right to burial after death according to his/her will, observing
customs and traditions, religious and cult rites. Burial is carried out taking into account religious
customs and traditions that do not contradict the generally recognized principles of morality and
ethics. Islam, which is professed by the majority of the population of the Kyrgyz Republic, has its own
attitude to the burial rite, its own rituals and rules for saying goodbye to the deceased.
161.   Regulation on Water Protective Zones and Strips of Water Sites waters by the Government
of the Kyrgyz Republic dated July 7, 1995, under resolution No. 271. This Regulation establishes
procedures for creating water protective zones and strips around water bodies to prevent pollution,
depletion, and siltation. Boundaries are defined by geographical and hydrogeological conditions,
water use, and local features, and are marked on maps. It also sets rules for land use and
responsibilities within these areas, outlining both inner and outer protective boundaries.
5.2 World Bank’s Environmental and Social Standards 5 on Land Acquisition, Restrictions
    on Land Use and Involuntary Resettlement
162.    The World Bank’s experience shows that when implemented without appropriate
implementation and mitigation measures, the resettlement works implemented under development
projects may result in impoverishment to the affected population. These problems are caused by
economic displacement due to loss of means of livelihoods, and by physical displacement due to loss
of land and houses. While part of the affected population may restore their livelihoods in a reasonable
timeframe, others may take longer time, particularly those are vulnerable, disadvantaged, or
marginalized.
5.2.1 The World Bank policy on land acquisition, restrictions on land use and involuntary
      resettlement (ESS5)
163.    Development physical and economic displacement, if unmitigated, may give rise to severe
economic, social and environmental risks: production systems may be dismantled; people face
impoverishment if their productive resources or other income sources are lost; people may be
relocated to environments where their productive skills are less applicable and the competition for
resources greater; community institutions and social networks may be weakened; kin groups may be
dispersed; and cultural identity, traditional authority, and the potential for mutual help may be
diminished or lost. For these reasons, involuntary resettlement should be avoided. Where involuntary
resettlement is unavoidable, it will be minimized and appropriate measures to mitigate adverse
impacts on displaced persons (and on host communities receiving displaced persons) will be carefully
planned and implemented.

                                                                                                                 39

                                                                                                         Official Use Only
  Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program         Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework
  164.     The WB’s ESS5 objectives are:
           -To avoid involuntary resettlement or, when unavoidable, minimize involuntary
            resettlement by exploring project design alternatives;
        - To avoid forced eviction19;
        - To mitigate unavoidable adverse social and economic impacts from land acquisition or
            restrictions on land use by: (a) providing timely compensation for loss of assets at
            replacement cost and (b) assisting displaced persons in their efforts to improve, or at least
            restore, their livelihoods and living standards, in real terms, to pre-displacement levels or
            to levels prevailing prior to the beginning of project implementation, whichever is higher;
        - To improve living conditions of poor or vulnerable persons who are physically displaced,
            through provision of adequate housing, access to services and facilities, and security of
            tenure;
        - To conceive and execute resettlement activities as sustainable development programs,
            providing sufficient investment resources to enable displaced persons to benefit directly
            from the project, as the nature of the project may warrant;
        - To ensure that resettlement activities are planned and implemented with appropriate
            disclosure of information, meaningful consultation, and the informed participation of
            those affected.
5.2.2 Required measures for better resettlement results
  165.  The following measures will help get better resettlement results
        - Consultation will be made with affected persons on proposed measures for compensation,
           support, and livelihood restoration and resettlement plans;
        - Affected persons will be provided with options for Livelihood Restoration and
           Resettlement;
        - Compensation for affected assets will be provided at full replacement costs;
        - Resettlement sites shall come with basic infrastructure and services similar to that of
           affected persons’ previous residential areas, or better;
        - Relocated persons will be provided with allowances, support during relocation process,
           and support in job training and job finding to restore their income generation activities;
        - Vulnerable groups will be identified and will be provided with additional support to assist
           them with their effort to restore their livelihoods.
5.2.3 Compensation Criteria and Eligibility
  166.      The eligibility for obtaining entitlements to compensation follow the principles below:
      (i)      Those who have formal legal rights to land or assets (including customary and traditional
               rights recognized under the laws of the country) – in this instance, it is also useful to
               document how long they have been using the land or the assets associated with it;
      (ii)     Those who do not have formal legal rights to land at the time the census begins but have
               a claim to such land or assets, provided that such claims are recognized under the laws of
               the country or become recognized through a process identified in the Livelihood
               Restoration and Resettlement Plan;
      (iii)    Those who have no recognizable legal right or claim to the land they are occupying; and

  19 “Forced eviction” is defined as the permanent or temporary removal against the will of individuals, families, and/or
  communities from the homes and/or land which they occupy without the provision of, and access to, appropriate forms
  of legal and other protection, including all applicable procedures and principles in this ESS.
                                                                                                                         40

                                                                                                                 Official Use Only
  Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program     Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework

      (iv)      Persons encroaching land after the cut-off date determined in the Project LRRP are not
                entitled to any compensation or assistance from the Project.
  167.    Those affected people of the type (i) and (ii) above shall be compensated for acquired land
  and other support. Those affected people of (iii) type will be supported with resettlement instead of
  compensation for the acquired land and other assistance, if necessary, to achieve the objectives set
  out in this policy, if they hold the land located in the Project area before the cut-off date specified in
  the Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement Plans (LRRPs). For affected people of (iv) type, no
  compensation and assistance is provided.
5.2.4 Livelihood restoration
  168.     ESS5 includes provision of timely assistance to restore livelihoods, particularly for vulnerable
  groups. Where livelihoods of displaced persons are land-based20 or where land is collectively owned,
  the Borrower will offer the displaced persons an option for replacement land. If no replacement land
  is available, assistance to PAPs with income affected should be provided in lieu of land compensation
  sufficient to reestablish livelihoods elsewhere.
5.2.5 Policy Gap analysis: Government of the Kyrgyz Republic regulations and WB ESF
  169.   There are various differences between the government’s regulations and WB’s ESS5 with
  regards to compensation, support, resettlement and livelihood rehabilitation for affected persons.
  The table below summarizes key differences between the two policies and measures proposed for
  the project to harmonize the requirements of both government and the WB’s ESS5.
  170. In case of gaps between the law of the Kyrgyz Republic and the requirements of ESS5, the
  principles and procedures of ESS5 shall apply.




  20 The term “land-based” includes livelihood activities such as grazing of livestock.

6 POTENTIAL IMPACTS
172.     Resettlement, land acquisition and other social impacts will take place in two phases:
i) for the dam construction site, early works and associated facilities; and ii) before the is
completed, for the reservoir impounded area and the water protection zone (WPZ). It may
takes 4.5 years to fill the reservoir. As indicated above, two stand-alone livelihood restoration
and resettlement plans will be prepared for these two phases.
6.1 Impacts related to the main construction site
173.     The construction site (i.e. dam and HPP) is located in a gorge of the Naryn river (See
Figures 2 and 4). It includes the following items: diversion tunnels, dam, powerhouse and
substation pre-excavation and site installation. The dam will be 256-meter-high with a 5.4
billion cubic meter reservoir.
6.1.1 Land Acquisition Issues
174.     The construction site is located in an area with no human activities due to the steep
slopes and the semi-desert vegetation (see Figure 2). The area needed for the main
construction site is estimated at 12.56 ha affecting two AAs : Toluk (6.87 ha) and E. Imanaliev
(5.69 ha). At the dam site, the land is mainly barren public land (10.66 ha, 84.9 %). Pastures
represent 1.90 ha (15.3%), almost all located in Toluk AA. There are no structures located at
this site..
175.     The Tables below summarizes the potential social and resettlement impacts related
to the construction site.

6.1.2 Location of workers’ camps
177.   The construction of the dam and other works will request a large number of workers
during 10 years with a peak of 3,000 workers. A significant proportion of the workers may be
migrant workers. Efforts should however be made to use local labor, especially for unskilled

                                                                                                                   49

                                                                                                                  Official Use Only
 Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework


 tasks. At the time of the LRRF preparation, the final location of the workers’ camp had not yet
 been determined. Possible locations include: i) Ketmen Tobo Village; ii) an affected village
 (Nickhe-Sai); or iii) the dam site.
 • Ketmen-Tobo
 178.   Ketmen-Tobo is an urban-type settlement located downstream of the Toktogul
 reservoir. It previously hosted a workers’ camp during the construction of the Kambarata-2
 Hydropower Plant (HPP) between 2007 and 2010. No social conflicts and no cases of sexual
 violence were reported during the K2-HPP construction.
 179.    Local authorities strongly support hosting the Kambarata-1 (K1) HPP workers’ camp,
 given its proximity, only 25 km from the K1 HPP construction site. They have identified
 available municipal-owned urban space suitable for the camp and existing public
 infrastructure, including a school, to accommodate workers and their families. However,
 some of these infrastructures require substantial renovation, and the municipality faces
 financial constraints in maintaining it.
 • Nickhe-Sai Village
 180.    During initial consultations, some local communities expressed interest in hosting the
 workers’ camp, viewing it as an opportunity for economic development. This perspective was
 particularly emphasized by local authorities in Nickhe-Sai, which is expected to be one of the
 most affected communities, with over 2,000 hectares, primarily pasture land, expected to be
 inundated. Local authorities believe that establishing the workers’ camp in Nickhe-Sai would
 provide economic benefits to the affected population and have identified available land for
 its construction.
 • Dam site
 181.     Locating the workers’ camp at the dam site or close to the dam site would eliminate
 the need for worker transportation and allow for greater control over the workforce due to
 its isolated location. However, this option presents significant challenges, as there is currently
 no existing infrastructure to accommodate workers and their families. Additionally, it may
 require a larger land acquisition to develop the necessary facilities.

 182.   The first LRRP on early works and associated facilities will cover the impacts related to
 the (one or more) workers’ camp(s) once its location(s) finalized.
 6.1.3 Labor influx
 183.    As indicated above, labor influx is expected to be significant, with a peak of 3,000
 workers, creating challenges for this low-density area. The location of the worker’s camp will
 be critical to reduce social conflicts.
 184.   The direct area of influence for the project has 13 villages with a population of
 approximately 15,000 villagers. At peak construction, the project is expected to employ 3,000
 workers, equivalent to 20% of the directly affected villages and 12% of the population in a
 broader area (i.e. four directly affected Aiyl Aimaks). Many positions require specialized
 technical skills, while most unskilled and trade jobs will be managed by subcontractors.
 185.    According to the socio-economic baseline, most local residents have completed
 secondary education, but fewer advance to the 11th grade or higher education. Even fewer
 pursue professional training. Skilled individuals from the area often migrate to cities such as
 Jalalabad, Osh, and Bishkek, or abroad to Russia and Turkey for better economic
 opportunities.
 186.    Given the significant workforce demand and the technical skill gap among local
 residents, a large influx of in-migrant workers is expected in the Project area. These individuals
 will come from various backgrounds and locations, including non-local relatives of residents,
 daily or weekly commuters, project employees, and contractors or subcontractors from
 outside the region or even other countries. With a large project footprint and a construction
 phase spanning 6 -7 years, the area may also attract additional migrants beyond the core
 workforce. These could include job seekers, family members of workers, service providers,
 including sex workers, opportunistic migrants, and fortune seekers hoping to find
 employment or business opportunities.
 187.    Most workers are expected to reside in (one or more) camps during the construction
 phase. The locations of these camps are still being assessed. Transportation, including buses,
 will be provided to shuttle workers between the camps and worksites.
 188.     The arrival of a large number of people in this sparsely populated area will have
 significant impacts on the local population. Typical adverse effects of such labor influx may
 include:
     - Cultural and Language Barriers: Workers from outside the area may not speak Kyrgyz
          and/or may not be familiar with local cultural practices, leading to tensions or conflict
          with community members
 189. Increased Crime and Security Concerns: The influx of workers and service providers
 may contribute to higher crime rates or a heightened perception of insecurity among locals.
 This could include theft, physical assaults, substance abuse, prostitution, and human
 trafficking. Local law enforcement may struggle to manage the increased demand for security.


                                                                                                                  51

                                                                                                                 Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework


    -    Strain on Public Services: A sudden rise in population can put significant pressure on
         local resources, including water, electricity, healthcare, transportation, education, and
         social services, especially if additional infrastructure is not developed to support the
         influx.
    -    Health Risks and Disease Transmission: The arrival of new workers may introduce
         communicable diseases, including sexually transmitted infections (STIs), to the area.
         In turn, incoming workers may also be exposed to local diseases for which they have
         little immunity. This can overwhelm local healthcare systems, particularly if workers
         seek anonymous treatment at local clinics rather than project medical facilities.
         Additionally, emergency services may be under-resourced to handle an increase in
         industrial accidents at the construction site.
    -    Economic Impacts: Increased demand for goods and services may lead to price
         inflation, making essential items less affordable for local residents.
    -    Traffic and Transportation Challenges: The movement of construction materials and
         workers can contribute to traffic congestion and a higher risk of road accidents, placing
         additional strain on transportation infrastructure.
    -    Workplace Discrimination and Harassment: Foreign workers and women may face
         discrimination and harassment, creating social tensions within the workforce and local
         communities.
190.     Considering the potential influx of migratory labor for the long-term construction of
the project, a Labor Influx Management Plan (LIMP) will be developed by the contractors
within one month of being awarded a contract and before beginning any activities. The LIMP
will include the following measures but not limited to:
    - Enforce labor policies: fair conditions, minimum age, equal pay
    - Develop and implement a Labor Management Procedure incorporating guidance on
         mental health, discrimination, employment contracts, and human resources.
    - Contractors to implement Labor Management Procedures & Code of Conduct
    - Secure, well-serviced worker accommodations with influx management
    - Biannual independent inspections for compliance with ILO standards
    - Safeguards against GBV/SEA/SH: well-lit areas, secure facilities, safe transport

estimated that 76.3 ha of land will be acquired (including 28.66 ha of pasture). A Due Diligence
Report (DDR) will be prepared for these early works to ensure that land acquisition and
resettlement, if any, was conducted in compliance with the proposed project policy. The DDR
will be part of the first LRRP (for the dam construction site early works and associated
facilities).

 6.2 Impacts related to the inundated area
 192.    The total area of the reservoir's inundation zone is approximately 61.7 km2 Most of
 the inundated area (5,792 ha, 94.6%) is located in Toktogul district, encompassing lands from
 the following AAs: Elmirbek Imanaliev: 2,648 ha, Sary-Kamysh: 1,580 ha and Toluk: 1,564 ha.
 The inundation zone also includes 332 ha within other AAs in Toguz-Toro and Jumgal districts.
 However, in this area, the anticipated increase in water level is minimal compared to the more
 significant changes expected in the Toktogul district (See Table 18).
 193.   In addition, according to the regulation on water protection zones and strips of water
 bodies in the Kyrgyz Republic21 for reservoir more than 1000 million cubic meters, a water
 protection zone (WPZ) of 500 m needs to be established. The WPZ will occupy an area of 8,262
 ha where activities will be restricted (see section 4.3).

 6.2.1 Project Affected Households/Persons
 194.    The estimated number of PAPs under the K1-HPP is estimated at 1,024 households
 (6,211 persons). Most of the affected households (1,021) are pasture users (mainly herders);
 in Atai AA, out of 25 AHs, three shepherds are also affected; these shepherds are responsible
 for collecting and grazing cattle and sheep from 22 AHs across the villages of Birdik, Aral, and
 Kazarman.
 195.     145 households have formal pasture tickets issued by Municipal Enterprises and 879
 are reported not having any pasture tickets. The three shepherds, in Atai AA, have pasture
 tickets.
 196.    As indicated in section 2.4.9, it is worth to note that the vast majority of pasture users
 (86.1%) do not have pastures tickets. However under the project policy, all pasture users with
 livelihood affected will be entitled to participate in the livelihood restoration program. If new
 pastures are allocated to them, thew will have to get a formal contract with the Municipal
 Enterprises

and n Jumgal district are less affected (377.7 ha, 6.1%)

6.2.3 Loss of Pastures
200.    It is estimated that around 3,531.5 ha of pastures will be inundated due to the
reservoir. These pastures are used by 1,021 HHs (6,195 persons) and 3 shepherds responsible
for collecting and grazing cattle and sheep from 22 households across the villages of Birdik,
Aral, and Kazarman. Most of the pastures affected are spring and autumn (1,858.7 ha, 52.6%).
Winter pasture represents 37.6% (1,329.5 ha) but are concentrated in Toluk and Sary-Kamysh.
In Toluk, winter pastures represent 83.4% of the total area of pastures lost. Winter pastures
are the most valuable pastures as they are critical for feeding animals during the winter
season. Summer pastures represents 9.7% (343.4 ha) and are present only in E. Imanaliev AA
and in some other AAs. Regarding Atai herders, impacts related to land leased/used lost, will
be addressed during LRRPs preparation.
201.    In addition to households, the local community of hunters who use lands allocated by
the Ministry of Natural Resources, where several thousand wild ungulates, birds and
predatory animals, including vulnerable species, live, will lose land. Flooding of individual
areas of pastures will affect herds of ungulates and small animals, which are the main trophic
link of the left bank ecosystem, which in turn will make adjustments to the order of top
predators.

206.   The loss of these paths will hinder movement to certain pastures. These paths are
mainly found along the lower parts of the mountains near the rivers. In particular, flooding of
the lower reaches of the Toluk River will impede access of Toluk residents to their winter
pasture located in the vicinity of Naryn river as they are using paths or tracks along the river.
These paths give access to remaining non-inundated winter pastures. Animals are led along
the Toluk River from the settled areas to the winter pasture at mid-slope along the Naryn
River.
207.   The inundation of the Toluk River valley would flood this route (see Figure 18). In
addition, there is a small bridge in Toluk AA, which is used to cross the Toluk River will be
submerged because of the Project. This bridge over the tributary, has long served as a vital
connection for Toluk residents.
208.   There is no alternative access since it is surrounded by a steep mountain that is
impossible to cross because of winter snow. The filling of the reservoir will also cut off access
to winter pastures in Sary-Kamysh AA (see Figure 19).
209.   The area of pastures that will become inaccessible and the number of pasture users
affected is not yet known but is expected to be significant especially in Toluk. Since winter
pastures are the most valuable and their replacement is highly challenging, the loss of access
could have critical adverse impacts on local livelihoods if not properly mitigated and managed.

6.2.5 Loss of Agriculture Land
211.     Approximately 2.99 hectares of agricultural land will be inundated once the reservoir
is filled. This includes 0.19 hectares in Sary-Kamysh AA and 2.8 hectares in E. Imanaliev AA. It
is likely that local people cultivate fodder/hay for the animal, vegetables and other crops and
fruit trees, for their own consumption, on public land.
212.    The number of PAPs with agriculture land remains undetermined at the time of this
report. However, given the local pasture use practices, it is expected that those utilizing this
land will be accounted for in the total number of pasture users affected by the reservoir area.
The census and inventory of losses to be conducted during LRRP preparation will confirm the
number of PAPs and the ownership of these lands.

214.    The potentially affected structures have been identified from satellite images and
through consultations with local people during primary data collection. It is estimated that a
total of 41 permanent or temporary structures/shelters and 13 secondary structures will be
affected by the reservoir and will become unusable for their current users. Among the 41
structures, 3 HHs living permanently in the inundated area will have to be physically displaced.

216.   During the IOL and census to be conducted during LRRP2 preparation, the legal status
of these households, will be investigated to provide adequate compensation/support in order
to ensure security of tenure.
6.2.7.2 Loss of temporary structures (shelters)
217.   41 temporary shelters used by herders during autumn/spring and summer will be fully
affected. These temporary structures can be classified in 3 categories: Tahm, Kepeh and Zherh
Toloh.
218.    Tahm is a small building (usually 4 x 2 meters) made of clay bricks and stones, usually
1-2 rooms, roof is covered with reeds, wood (tree branches) and polyethylene plastic sheet
or tarpaulin, metal nails and wires are used to fix roof frame. It has one door with a padlock,
and one or two small windows covered with a glass or transparent plastic sheet. On it's floor,
plastic sheets and reeds are used as floor covers. It also has in-door chimney and primitive
fireplace for cooking. This setup allows the cattle owners to temporarily live and cook
comfortably while tending to their herds.
219.    Kepeh or Zhehr Toloh is a construction dug into ground usually 1room (about 2x 3
meters), roof is made of roof is covered with reeds, wood (tree branches) and polyethylene
plastic sheet or tarpaulin, metal nails and wires are used to fix roof frame. It has one door with
a padlock, and sometimes one small window covered with transparent plastic sheet. Some
clay and stone are used to elevate sides / walls of the construction.
220.     We should note that these temporary structures are used by several different families.

    Table 22: Quantity of Potentially Affected Temporary House/Shelter (Inundated Ara)

6.2.9 Loss of Cultural Assets
225.   The inundated area contains graves and burial grounds, as confirmed by communities.
There are old graveyards (9) in the low areas of the villages which may get impacted due to
submergence. Two (2) cultural sites will also be affected (See Table 24 and Figures 24 and 25).
226.    According to the department of disease prevention and health of the Toktugul district,
the reservoir area includes remains of old rural cemeteries where people lived in these areas
in the 1820-1870s. During the Soviet period (1920-1930), people were resettled to permanent
residence in the villages of Ozgorush, Nichke-Sai, etc. From 1974 to 1994, and also from 1994
to the present day, these places were not used as rural cemeteries. The last burial was made
in 1984.
227.   Moving and reburial of graves need to comply with existing regulations and religious
customs. During public meetings, the sensitivity of moving graves has been highlighted. Any
grave displacement will need to be discussed with local population.
228.   As indicated in Chapter 2, a number of regulation restricts the burial and reburial
process25. Displacement and reburial of graves is under the jurisdiction of department of
health of districts. The displacement and reburial will have to follow sanitary criteria indicated
in the regulations. The removal options and a new area will be decided by responsible
authority according to the national legislation in consultation with the affected PAPs/
communities, and in coordination with local elderly and religious leaders.

6.2.10 Loss of Livelihoods
6.2.10.1            Livestock herding
229.    The 1,021 herders that will be affected through loss of pastures have a total of 21,950
livestock, mainly sheep and goats (17,800 heads, 81.1%), followed by cattle (2,760, 12.6%)
and horses (1,390, 6.3%). These herders will have to move their livestock to other pastures in
order to keep their livelihoods.
