                                                                                                                 67

                                                                                                                Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework


230.   In March 2025, Pasture Management Consultants carried out a site visit and technical
meetings with local herders were carried out to assess the impact of the reservoir on pasture
use. Several key concerns emerged during these discussions, which have to be taken into
account when developing livelihood restoration measures.
• Toluk AA:
    -    According to consultation with the local authorities, up to 6,000 hectares of non-
         inundated pastures may become inaccessible due to the loss of a key bridge over the
         tributary, which connects Toluk residents to their winter pastures. According to local
         authorities, winter pastures are irreplaceable in Toluk, as no other similar quality
         pastures non-inundated (capable to feed the animals during the winter time) are
         available in the area.
    -    Replacing these winter pastures remains highly challenging. Even with improved
         access roads, concerns persist about whether the remaining pastures will be sufficient
         to support livestock. A geobotanical study is currently being conducted to assess the
         carrying capacity of the remaining grazing areas.
    -    Several potential solutions have been discussed, including preparing hay for winter
         feeding, stable-based breeding and sheltering livestock in barns, sending livestock to
         summer pastures to ease the pressure on winter grazing, transitioning from cows to
         yaks. However, all these options are costly, complex, and may not fully compensate
         for the loss of winter pastures.
    -    Given the severity of this issue, a comprehensive pasture capacity assessment is
         required to determine whether alternative grazing areas can offset the loss of winter
         pastures. Additionally, technical feasibility studies must be conducted in close
         collaboration with experts to explore solutions for restoring access to these lands. A
         detailed evaluation of the costs, practicality, and impact of different mitigation
         measures is necessary.
    -    Since this is a primary concern for Toluk residents, it is essential to prioritize the
         technical assessment and develop a mitigation plan for restoring access to winter
         pastures.
• E. Imanaliev AA:
    -    In E. Imanaliev AA, while a significant portion of pasture land will be submerged by the
         reservoir, the affected areas primarily consist of summer and spring-autumn pastures.
         Given the availability of these pasture types in the region, the impact on pasture-based
         livelihoods is less severe compared to Toluk AA.
    -    During consultations with local authorities, some proposals were made to address
         pasture access, aside from constructing a 100 km road to Santa Polo and John Terek
         summer pastures. However, this proposal may face opposition from the John Terek
         hunting company, which has raised concerns about potential disturbances to wildlife.
    -    To support pasture livelihoods in Imanaliev AA, recommended measures include
         transitioning to alternative livelihoods, such as developing tourism and establishing
         sewing workshops for women to create additional income opportunities. Additionally,
         infrastructure improvements, particularly providing electricity to barns, could help
         facilitate year-round pasture use.

                                                                                                                 68

                                                                                                                Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework


• Sary-Kamysh AA:
    -    When discussing the loss of winter pastures, local stakeholders in Sary-Kamysh AA did
         not express major concern because they see other grazing alternatives. While losing
         pastures is not ideal, they believe that with proper support and access improvements,
         they can adapt to other available grazing lands.
    -    To address these concerns, stakeholders emphasized the importance of negotiating
         with forestry authorities to obtain land for grazing. Additionally, constructing new
         roads to provide access to alternative pastures was seen as a crucial solution. Once
         these roads are in place, the loss of current winter pastures can be mitigated, allowing
         communities to continue their livestock practices with minimal disruption
231.   In addition to the specific issues identified for each AA, several broader challenges may
complicate the development of the land acquisition process:
    -    During the socio-economic surveys in November 2024, local residents highlighted
         ongoing conflicts between villages over pasture use. In addition, disputes exist
         between pasture committees, the forestry service, and national state parks over
         several pasture areas across the affected AAs. These conflicts may create obstacles for
         the Project when verifying actual pasture users and determining eligibility for
         compensation.
    -    Due to the nomadic nature of many pasture users, direct interviews were difficult to
         conduct, leading to reliance on village leaders for representation. However, this
         approach risks overlooking individual perspectives and specific needs. Some proposed
         solutions may not align with the practical realities of pastoral communities, as many
         suggestions come from individuals who do not directly use the pastures.
    -    In public meetings, community elders and leaders were nominated to speak on behalf
         of villagers, as many residents lacked the technical knowledge to articulate complex
         proposals. While this approach was considered democratic and culturally appropriate,
         village leaders often requested additional time to formulate and refine their
         community’s needs due to the legal weight of formally documented requests.
232.    These factors underscore the importance of a careful and inclusive consultation
process to ensure that individual needs and perspectives are accurately captured in the
resettlement planning.
6.2.10.2          Beekeeping
233.    The inundation of pasture land due to the reservoir will also further exacerbate the
challenges already faced by beekeepers in the affected communities. Given that 5% to 20% of
households in the affected AAs engage in beekeeping, with higher dependence in certain
villages like Toluk (where it contributes up to 30% of household incomes), the loss of pasture
land and associated vegetation will directly impact honey production.
234.   The submergence of flowering plants, wild herbs, and natural ecosystems critical for
bee foraging will reduce nectar availability, potentially leading to lower honey yields and
increased competition for remaining foraging sites.
235.   Additionally, environmental disturbances caused by reservoir filling, including changes
in microclimates and potential dust pollution from construction activities, may stress bee
colonies, leading to migration or decreased productivity.

                                                                                                                 69

                                                                                                                Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework


6.2.10.3          Hunting grounds
236.    Regarding land use for hunting, a representative of the hunting company indicated
that, in his view, the construction of K1-HPP would not have a significant negative impact on
the hunting grounds. The only concern raised was the increased risk of poaching: once the
reservoir is filled, previously inaccessible areas may become reachable by boat, making it
easier for poachers to enter.
6.2.10.4          Fishing
237.    As indicated above, fishing activities are concentrated in the Toktogul reservoir. A
small number of villagers is fishing in the Naryn river but for their own consumption and small
scale. Fishing will be authorized in the future reservoir and could constitute an additional
livelihood activity for herders. Therefore, no adverse impacts are anticipated

6.3 Impacts related to the Water Protection Zones
6.3.1       Protection Zones
238.   As indicated above, according to the Kyrgyz regulations, protection zones apply around
the reservoir. Two main protection zones will apply for the Kambarata 1 reservoir: i) Water
Protection Zone and ii) Sanitary Protection Zone.
6.3.1.1     Water Protection Zone
239.   In coordination with the authorized state environmental protection body and local
state administration bodies, the State Water Administration establishes water protection
zones around water bodies26, where certain types of activities are restricted or prohibited. For
reservoirs more than 1000 million cubic meters, a water protection zone (WPZ) of 500 m
needs to be established. The water protection zone will occupy an area of 8,262 ha around
the reservoir.
240.   A WPZ is defined as the area adjacent to a water body where a special regime is
enforced to prevent pollution, littering, depletion, and siltation. This regime directly affects
pasture users, as it prohibits several activities within this WPZ, including:
- placement of livestock complexes and farms, poultry farms, wastewater storage facilities
  from livestock complexes, irrigation systems using prepared manure-containing
  wastewater, cattle burial grounds, burial and storage sites for manure, garbage and
  industrial waste dumps, mechanical workshops, as well as maintenance, fueling and
  washing points for vehicles and agricultural machinery;
- cutting down of trees and shrubs, with the exception of forest thinning & sanitary cutting;
- application of fertilizers over snow cover, use of untreated manure-containing
  wastewater as fertilizer, use of persistent organochlorine pesticides;
- washing wool and soaking leather;
- construction of sheep dips and grazing of livestock, especially on elements of drainage &
  girder networks;
- performance of other types of work that have a harmful effect on the state of water
  bodies.

26Decree on water protection zones and strips of water bodies in the Kyrgyz Republic dated July 7, 1995 N

271(As amended by the Resolutions of the Government of the Kyrgyz Republic dated September 25, 2017 No.
606, Cabinet of Ministers of the Kyrgyz Republicfrom December 17, 2021 No. 307,July 29, 2022 No. 403)

                                                                                                                 70

                                                                                                                Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program      Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework


241.   In addition to the above restrictions, the following are also not permitted in the coastal
water protection zone: i) organization of summer cattle camps; ii) placement of recreation
centers, tent cities; iii) camps for hunters and tourists; and iv) parking and washing of vehicles
and agricultural machinery.
242.   Within the WPZ the following activities are permitted:
- Placement of fish breeding facilities: fish hatcheries, spawning and rearing facilities, fish
  breeding and fish farms, fish-rearing stations, artificial spawning grounds, fish-passage
  canals and hydraulic structures located outside the boundaries of specially protected
  natural areas, subject to a positive conclusion from the state environmental assessment;
- Placement of temporary fish-breeding facilities made of light structures for fish farming,
  fishing, fish breeding and reproduction of fish stocks: incubation shops with apparatus for
  incubating eggs and growing fish seed material, with subsequent release into fishery
  waters for the purpose of preserving and increasing fish stocks; fish-breeding inventory
  and equipment, netting materials, trays and pools; storage of fish feed and small vessels.
243.     The WPZ regime restricts grazing. However, experience with the Toktogul reservoir
shows that grazing continues in the WPZ. Compliance with the prohibition on grazing is
difficult to enforce, as monitoring this process is challenging. In most cases, the ban is not
upheld due to the absence of artificial or natural barriers or identification signs27. In practice,
grazing takes place without limitations, and even during dry years, when the water recedes,
local communities cultivate the exposed lands for agricultural purposes. However, a
confirmation is needed from relevant authorities that grazing will be authorized in the WPZ.
This is an important issue that needs to be clarified before the K1 filling dam process begins
during LRRP preparation.


6.3.1.2       Sanitary Protection Zone
244.   Sanitary protection zones (SPZ) are established around water bodies and water mana-
gement structures used for water abstraction from surface and groundwater for drinking and
household water supply, as well as for medicinal purposes. SPZ occupies territories around
the respective water management structure, including the water body itself.
245.   The location, size, and regime of the SPZs are determined by the relevant local state
administration body based on the basin plan and a developed project approved by the
authorized sanitary-epidemiological body.
246.   It is likely that the SPZ is included in the WPZ of 500 m and therefore, the LRRF will
only consider the impacts related to the WPZ.
6.3.2         Impacts on Land
247.   The WPZ will occupy an area of 9,644.6 ha; land use is divided in public barren land
(4,961.5 ha, 51.4%) and in pastures (4,683.1 ha, 48.6%)

6.3.3 Impacts on Pastures
248.    A total of 4,683.1 ha of pastures will be located within the WPZ (500 m around the
reservoir). This is more than in the reservoir area (3,538.5). Spring-Autumn pastures will be
the most affected (1,956.3 ha, 41.8%) followed by winter pastures, the most valuable (1,541.4
ha, 32.9%) and summer pastures (1,185.4 ha, 25.3%). As indicated above, land use will be
restricted in the WPZ and it is not clear if grazing will be authorized in the WPZ. Prohibiting
grazing in the water protection zone will therefore have a very severe impact for herders who
are already losing pastures in the inundated area. This is an important issue that needs to be
clarified before the K1 filling dam process begins.
249.    According to Pasture Management Consultant report, a total restriction on public
access to grazing resources within the 500 m water protection zone may cause discontent
among herders. Additionally, the availability of water resources within AA for watering farm
animals should be considered. If no water sources are located near grazing areas, shepherds
will inevitably bring their cattle to the reservoir for watering
250.   Discussion will be held with relevant water bodies during LRRP2 preparation to discuss
the conditions to authorize grazing and access to the reservoir by animals, in the WPZ, in order
to reduce impacts on herders.

6.3.4 Impacts on structures

designated WPZ. As in the inundation zone, structures are related to grazing such as Kepeh or
Zhehr Toloh and livestock pens. Discussion will be held with relevant water bodies to assess if
these structures have to be removed from the WPZ or not.

6.3.6 Impacts on Livelihoods
254.    As indicated above, the severity of impacts on livelihoods, are related to the extent of
restrictions to be imposed in the WPZ. If all grazing activities are prohibited, it will lead to
additional livelihood impacts to the pasture users in the reservoir and additional difficulties
to find other pastures to replace the pastures located in the WPZ.
255.    As indicated above, discussion should be held by K1-PMO with the water bodies to
identify conditions authorize grazing in the WPZ in order to minimize impacts on livelihoods.

7 COMPENSATION AND SUPPORT STRATEGY
7.1 Principles and Objectives Governing Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement
    Preparation and Implementation
257.   The section below summarizes key principles and objectives to be applied for persons
affected in terms of economic displacement and physical displacement under the Kambarata
1 - HPP. These principles are based on the World Bank’s ESS5 and the existing national laws
and regulations of the Kyrgyz Republic. These principles will be applied as a minimum
requirement to prepare and implement the two Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement
Plans. Additional benefits may be provided to affected households based on national
regulations at the time of preparing and implementing LRRPs.
258.     The following principles and objectives will be applied:
       − Involuntary resettlement and livelihood impacts must be avoided or, when
         unavoidable, involuntary resettlement must be minimized by exploring project
         design alternatives.
       − All PAPs residing, farming or grazing in the project area will be provided with
         rehabilitation measures, sufficient enough for them to improve or at least maintain
         their living standards, income earning and production capacity the same as their pre-
         Project conditions.
       − Lack of legal rights to acquired land will not prevent PAPs from their entitlement to
         access such rehabilitation measures.
       − Plans for land acquisition and other assets and provision of rehabilitation measures
         must be taken under the consultation in a culturally appropriate manner with PAPs
         to minimize their disturbance.
       − Entitlements shall be provided to PAPs prior to the expected commencement of
         works or the impoundment of the respective Project areas.
       − Existing public services and infrastructure shall be maintained or improved.
       − Budget for Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement shall be available in the Project
         implementation stages.
       − The implementing agency must ensure that the design, planning, consultation and
         implementation of the LRRPs is effectively and timely.
       − All PAPs who have permanent or temporarily assets within or reside within the area
         of project land acquisition before the cut-off date are entitled to compensation or
         support for their losses as per LRRPs. Those who have lost their income and/or
         subsistence will be eligible for livelihood restoration assistance based on the criteria
         of eligibility defined by the Project in consultation with the PAPs. If, by the end of
         the Project, livelihoods have been shown not to be restored to pre-Project levels,
         additional measures should be considered.
       − Agricultural land with ownership documents that is lost will be compensated in cash
         at the full replacement cost. These PAPs will be assisted in their livelihood
         subsistence.
       − Loss of pastures and economic displacement will be supported through allocation of
         new pastures or livelihood restoration measures (for example, employment,
         training, small business support, assistance in providing replacement pasture land

                                                                                                                 75

                                                                                                                Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework


          of equivalent productive value, other) available to persons losing pasture land or
          access to resources. These livelihood restoration measures can also include
          measures to promote improvement of productivity or incomes or incomes among
          displacement persons or communities,
        − Compensation for all permanent or temporarily residential or other structures will
          be offered at the replacement cost, without any depreciation of the structure and
          without deduction for salvageable materials.
        − The PAPs will be compensated in cash for transportation cost related to transporting
          personal belongings and assets to a new resettlement place.
        − Civil works or the impoundment of the respective project areas cannot start until
          land is cleared with the satisfaction of the Bank.

7.2 Eligibility Criteria

7.2.1 Cut-off date
259.   The MoE will establish a cut-off date for eligibility. The cut-off date is the day of the
beginning of the census. Two cut off dates will be established: i) one for the construction
sites, and early works and associated facilities and ii) one for the reservoir impounded area
and the WPZ.
260.   Information regarding the cut-off dates will be well documented and will be
disseminated throughout the project area at regular intervals in written and (as appropriate)
non-written forms and in relevant local languages. This will include posted warnings that
persons settling in the project area after the cut-off dates may be subject to removal.

7.2.2 Project affected people (PAP)
•      Individuals/families
261.     People directly affected by the project are:
       - Users of agricultural/pasture land that will be affected (permanently or temporarily)
         by the Project including loss of access to these agriculture/pasture lands;
       - Persons whose livelihood will be affected (permanently or temporarily) by the Project
         (pastures users i.e. livestock herders, shepherds and beekeepers; persons engaged in
         the collection of forest products etc.); and
       - Persons whose non-land assets (including graves) or access to those assets will be
         affected in part or in total by the Project;
•      Community/state assets, facilities
       - Community assets and facilities (access roads, horse paths, small bridges, etc.) that will
         be temporarily/permanently affected by the project;
       - Land, houses, structures of state that will be temporarily/permanently affected by the
         project;
•      Disadvantaged/ vulnerable individuals and groups
262.   Vulnerable groups are those who may be more likely to be adversely affected by the
project impacts and/or more limited than others in their ability to take advantage of a
project’s benefits. Vulnerable groups are identified in Art. 4 of the Law of the Kyrgyz Republic

                                                                                                                 76

                                                                                                                Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework


of December 19, 2001 No. 111 "On the Fundamentals of Social Services to the Population in
the Kyrgyz Republic.
263.    Vulnerable groups under the project may include the following categories, among
others: 1) Low-income households and individuals; 2) The elderly, people of pre-retirement
age; 3) People with disabilities; 4) Women head of households with low income.
264.   The LRRPs will establish the final list of vulnerable under the Project following
socioeconomic surveys and consultations.
7.3 Eligibility
265.   The eligibility for entitlement to compensation is determined according to the cut-off
date and by asset ownership criteria:
  (i)     Those who have formal legal rights to land or assets;
  (ii)    Those who do not have formal legal rights to land or assets, but have a claim to land
          or assets that is recognized or recognizable under national law (i.e. herders with
          pastures tickets issued by municipal enterprises/pasture committees to use pastures);
  (iii)   Those who have no recognizable legal right or claim to the land or assets they occupy
          or use (i.e. herders without pastures tickets issued by municipal enterprises/pasture
          committees to use pastures but living stably since a long period in the project area);
266.    Persons covered under (i) and (ii) are provided compensation or support for the land
they lose, and other livelihood restoration assistance. Persons covered under (iii) are provided
resettlement and/or livelihood restoration assistance in lieu of compensation for the land
they occupy, and other assistance, as necessary, to achieve the objectives set out in this policy,
if they occupy the project area prior to the cut-off dates established by the MoE and
acceptable to the Bank. All are entield to comepnsation for loss of improvements made to the
land(crops, structures, irrigation works) as per the entitlement matrix.
267.      Persons who encroach on the area after the relevant cut-off date are not entitled to
compensation or any other form of resettlement assistance. All persons included in (i), (ii), or
(iii) are provided compensation for loss of owned or used assets other than land.

7.4 Entitlement
268.   With respect to a particular eligibility category, entitlements are the sum of
compensations and other forms of assistance provided to PAPs. Entitlements for each
category of loss are detailed in the Entitlement Matrix (see Table 32).

7.5      Methods of valuing affected land and assets

7.5.1 Approach

269.    Methods used for the valuation of losses in WB funding Projects are based on full
replacement costs. Where functioning markets exist, replacement cost is the market value as
established through independent and competent real estate valuation, plus transaction costs.
270.   In all instances where physical displacement results in loss of shelter, replacement
cost must at least be sufficient to enable purchase or construction of housing that meets
acceptable minimum community standards of quality and safety. In circumstances where a
house being relocated is of sub-standard quality, full replacement cost is not sufficient by
paying for the sub-standard housing; compensation would need to be enough to ensure the
house meets minimum quality standards as acceptable to the community.
271.   Transaction costs include administrative charges, registration or title fees, reasonable
moving expenses, and any similar costs imposed on affected persons. To ensure
compensation at replacement cost, planned compensation rates may require updating in
project areas where inflation is high or the period of time between calculation of
compensation rates and delivery of compensation is extensive.
272.    The aim of full replacement cost compensation is to improve, or at least restore,
affected people’s livelihoods and living standards, in real terms, to pre-displacement levels or
to levels prevailing prior to the beginning of project implementation, whichever is higher, as
well as to improve living conditions of poor or vulnerable persons who are physically
displaced, through provision of adequate housing, access to services and facilities, and
security of tenure.
273.   In determining the replacement cost, depreciation of the asset and the value of
salvage materials are not taken into account, nor is the value of benefits to be derived from
the project deducted from the valuation of an affected asset.
7.5.2 In the Kyrgyz Republic (KR), methods for valuation of assets are based on the Land
         Code of the KR dated July 18, 2025, No. 149 and on the Civil Code of the KR, Parts I
         and II (May 8, 1996, No. 15 and January 5, 1998, No. 1).
7.5.3 Replacement costs survey
274.    As required by the World Bank’s ESS5 on Involuntary Resettlement, Replacement Cost
Survey (RCS) will need to be carried out to establish the basis for calculation of replacement
costs for all the lands/crops/pastureland/structures/assets affected by the Project. In relation
to this, RCS needs to be completed to establish compensation rates for all LRRPs that are
prepared under the Project.
275.    K1-PMO will contract an independent valuator to establish replacement costs
according to the Law of the KR “On Appraisal Activity” dated December 22, 2021, No. 155.
This Law establishes a legal basis and regulates relations arising in connection with appraisal
activity in the KR. It sets forth the requirement to carry out independent appraisal in case of
forced expropriation of a property for state or public needs, involving payment of
compensation. In accordance with the Law, an individual, certified appraiser or a legal entity
having no less than two certified appraisers, may engage in appraisal activities. Like the Land
Code, the Law stipulates that cadastral value of a land plot shall be used in calculation and

                                                                                                              84

                                                                                                              Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework


payment of losses and damages caused by land acquisition for state and public needs. ToR for
the independent valuator will be prepared as part of LRRPs preparation.
276.   Calculation of compensation will be carried out based on the above replacement costs
and on the Entitlement Matrix approved as part of this LRRF.
7.6 Forced eviction
277.    “Forced eviction” is defined as the permanent or temporary removal against the will
of individuals, families, and/or communities from the homes and/or land which they occupy
without the provision of, and access to, appropriate forms of legal and other protection,
including all applicable procedures and principles in the ESS5. The exercise of eminent
domain, compulsory acquisition or similar powers by local authorities will not be considered
to be forced eviction providing it complies with the requirements of national law and the
provisions of the ESS5, and is conducted in a manner consistent with basic principles of due
process (including provision of adequate advance notice, meaningful opportunities to lodge
grievances and appeals, and avoidance of the use of unnecessary, disproportionate or
excessive force).
278.    The prohibition against “forced eviction” does not prevent action by local authorities
to remove a person who continues to occupy land upon completion of the legal process of
eminent domain or compulsory acquisition. Eviction is not considered to be forced eviction if
it conforms to national law, including compliance with, and completion of, all relevant legal
and administrative procedures, including appeals processes; complies with all the relevant
requirements of ESS5, and is carried out in a way that respects basic principles of due process.
279.   Monitoring and Evaluation: The K1-PMO must report the forced eviction to the MoE
and the World Bank to review before implementing the process. Reporting should be carried
out on a regular basis and submitted to relevant authorities and WB’s for review and
agreement.
280.    Independent monitoring by third party should be carried out to ensure the forced
eviction process conforms to national laws and regulations, including compliance with, and
completion of, all relevant legal and administrative procedures, including appeals processes;
complies with all the relevant requirements of ESS5, and is carried out in a way that respects
basic principles of due process.




                                                                                                              85

                                                                                                              Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement
Framework



8 LIVELIHOOD RESTORATION STRATEGY
8.1 Objectives of the Livelihood restoration Program, part of the Livelihood Restoration
    and Resettlement Plan (LRRP)
281.    Households experiencing significant loss of productive resources, in particular loss of
pastures, are expected to face challenges in restoring their livelihoods, income generating capacity
and living standards. For vulnerable households, there is an opportunity within the project to
contribute to improving living standards.
282.   The purpose of the Livelihood Restoration Program, as part of the LRRP, is to assist
households losing pastures and other types of livelihoods to restore their income generating
capacity to at least pre-project levels.
8.2 Principles of Livelihood Restoration Program as part of the LRRP
283.     The general principles of LRRP planning and implementation are as follows:
-        Transparency: Information related to LRRP planning and implementation (eligible
         households, benefits, support levels, household contributions, etc.) must be fully disclosed
         to IRP participants.
-        Gender sensitivity: The planning, implementation and monitoring of IRP activities must
         include the participation of women.
-        Combination with existing or planned projects/programs in the localities: LRRP will
         combine with ongoing or planned projects/programs from the financial resources of the
         Government and non-governmental organization.
-        Participation in planning and implementation: Households entitled to LRRP will be
         facilitated to participate in all LRRP processes (planning, implementation, monitoring).
-        Appropriate livelihood development activities: It will be feasible, affordable and suitable to
         the needs of households, especially vulnerable households. Risk assessment and mitigation
         measures will be considered for each income restoration activity.
-        Comply with the Government's regulations on financial management: Cost estimation,
         disbursement and finalization will comply with the Government's regulations on financial
         management.
-        Flexibility: The program must be flexible because there are various changes in the
         implementation process, which can affect the outcome of the LRRP from the design and
         implementation steps. This flexibility needed to ensure that risks are minimized and the
         necessary resources are well mobilized and improvements are made to be identified and
         tailored to meet the needs of different groups due to condition change in implementation
         progress.
284.     Other features of the LRRP are presented below:
-        LRRP is based on voluntary participation of participants. Households who do not wish to
         participate will be asked to sign an application form to confirm this;
-        Do not provide cash to households for livelihood support. LRRP support is through the
         provision of inputs in pasture management (such as breeding stock/livestock/related
         materials), vocational training, and a cashless program for affected households.


                                                                                                                       86

                                                                                                                   Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program          Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework



-        LRRP implementation, households are not transferable. Households are not allowed to
         transfer inputs to other households for them to participate in the LRRP. Households are also
         not allowed to sell inputs received from the program, unless the end of the production cycle
         is reached and they must sell inputs to reinvest and run selected models over the life of IRP.
8.3 Households entitled to participate in Income Restoration Program
285.  According to the Project's Entitlement Matrix, entitled households to participate in the
Income Restoration Program include:
-     All pasture users losing pastures or access to pastures including hunters, beekeepers and
      non-timber forest collectors;
-        Vulnerable households, including: 1) Low-income households and individuals; 2) The elderly,
         people of pre-retirement age; 3) People with disabilities; 4) Women headed low-income HHs
8.4 Replacement of Lost Pastures
286.    Allocation of other pastures of the same category (i.e. winter, spring-autumn and summer)
and in the same area to affected pasture users is the preferred solution for herders as they could
continue their current livelihood.
287.     In addition to the proposals gathered from local communities during consultations, field
observations and analysis of official data on livestock numbers and pasture availability indicate that,
overall, the pilot area has a sufficient amount of pasture resources, particularly during the summer
season. According to the Pasture Management Report, the core issue is not the shortage of land
itself, but rather the limited accessibility of remote pasture plots and the uneven seasonal
distribution of grazing pressure.
288.    However, the situation changes drastically during winter. Following the implementation of
the Kambarata-1 Hydropower Plant project and the inundation of 3,538.5 hectares of pasture land,
including 1,541.4 hectares of critical winter pastures, and the possible restriction of grazing in the
WPZ, the traditional grazing system will face a serious sustainability challenge. As indicated, the
majority of grazing pressure falls on winter pastures, and the loss of a large portion of these areas
will make a shift in pasture use patterns unavoidable.
289.   In order to allocate other pastures, the first step is to assess the capacity of existing pastures
to accommodate more animals. Identification of new pastures to be allocated to herders has also
to be assessed.
290.   In case no other pasture (in particular winter pastures) is available in the same area to
replace lost pastures and if the livelihood of the pasture users will be significantly affected, at the
request of the PAP, the MoE will consider the physical displacement of the PAP and the allocation
of pastures in an other area. The RP to be prepared for the inundated area, will identify the PAPs
that could benefit from this option. Support for relocation will be provided to these HHs.
8.4.1      Pasture Capacity
291.   In order to estimate the capacity of remaining pastures to accommodate more animals,
Pasture Management Consultant30 calculated the pasture carrying capacity (in Livestock Units31)
with the following formula (A × B) / 7.5 / C) × 0.7, where:

30
   Pasture Management Consultant (2025) Supporting Kambarata 1 HPP PMO for the Livelihood Restoration and
Pastureland aspects preparing the Kambarata 1 HPP
31 livestock unit" (LU) is a reference unit that facilitates the aggregation of livestock from various species and age groups
using specific coefficients, initially based on the nutritional or feed requirements of each animal type
                                                                                                                          87

                                                                                                                     Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program      Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework



    -    A – Pasture area (ha);
    -    B – Pasture productivity (edible biomass, kg/ha);
    -    C – Number of grazing days;
    -    7.5 – Average daily feed requirement for one LU (kg);
    -    0.7 – Coefficient of forage utilization.

292.    In this report, the carrying capacity of each types of pastures in the three AA was assessed
based on the above formula. Then, an assessment of the pasture load was carried out using actual
livestock numbers (in LU), as shown in Table 33. For each Aiyl Aimak (AA), the ratio of livestock to
seasonal carrying capacity was calculated. This Table shows how many animals can be
accommodated within the available pastures, and what is the current grazing pressure.

    -    In Sary-Kamysh AA, elevated pressure is observed on summer pastures (1.47 LU/ha). This is

         overall grazing intensity.

         and growing competition for grazing resources.

295.   At the time of the preparation of the LRRP for the inundated area, a complete assessment

8.4.2 Identification of other Pastures
8.4.2.1 Pastures on Slopes Less than 35 degrees
296.     According to the Methodology for Conducting Geobotanical Surveys of Natural Forage
Lands, Preparing Large-Scale Maps, and Updating Geobotanical Survey Materials, approved by
Order No. 256 of the Ministry of Agriculture, Water Resources and Regional Development of the
Kyrgyz Republic dated August 2, 2021, pastures located on slopes steeper than 35 degrees are
classified as inaccessible.
297.   The map of pastures in the Toluk Aiyl Aimak shows that above the inundation zone there
are winter pastures with slopes less than 35 degrees, which are suitable for continued use.
However, access to these areas will be lost, raising concerns among local pasture users. In response,
they propose constructing a road along the mountain slopes to maintain access to the remaining
pastures.
8.4.2.2 Land Distribution Issues
298.    As indicated in section 2.4.13, due to the earthquake risks in the area, the government
promised land to residents of Toluk AA in the Chui region. This issue remains unresolved until now.
National Government support is essential to reach a positive resolution and enable the community
to access the land that was once allocated to them. While it may not be easy for the older
generation, who may have to abandon their livestock-based economy, to move to another area,
this would allow younger generations, who currently live in areas with limited economic
opportunities, to build better lives closer to urban centers, where there is more room for self-
realization and employment. Moving to the Chui region remains an option and herders can remain
on site if they wish.
299.    A similar concern was raised by residents of Sary-Kamysh AA, who also reported that 17
hectares of land in the Chui region had been allocated to them. This is likely due to the historical
fact that both Toluk and Sary-Kamysh AAs were once part of the same administrative unit.
300.      In addition, residents of Sary-Kamysh highlighted several land disputes, including:

8.4.3 Restore Access to Pastures
302.    During meetings with local authorities, a number of infrastructures were proposed to
restore access to pastures. In Toluk AA, four proposals were made. In Sary-Kamysh, two projects
were identified: the construction of a large bridge and a road along the future reservoir, providing
access to the pastures of Kichi and Chon Sary-Bulak, as well as Togorok.
303.    In E. Imanaliev AA, a significant portion of pasture land will be submerged by the reservoir,
the affected areas primarily consist of summer and spring-autumn pastures. Given the availability
of these pasture types in the region, the impact on pasture-based livelihoods is less severe
compared to Toluk AA
304.    Based on these proposals, a technical and economic assessment was made. Following this
assessment, ten infrastructure projects were identified (see Table 35) that are directly aimed at
mitigating the impacts of changes in livestock grazing practices resulting from the construction of
the Kambar-Ata-1 Hydropower Plant. Of these, two are bridge structures, and the remaining
projects are mountain roads intended to restore access to alternative pasture areas.

8.5 Building the capacity of sustainable grazing and veterinary services
307.   This section provides institutional measures (i.e. revise grazing calendar, development of
pasture management plan, artificial insemination program, agro-processing facilities in order to
enhance capacity building of local agencies (municipal enterprises, pasture committees,
veterinaries, agro-processing companies etc.).
8.5.1 Revise Grazing calendar
308.   Spring, summer, and autumn pastures are currently underutilized, offering potential for
optimization. It is recommended to revise the grazing calendar, for example, by keeping livestock
on autumn pastures slightly longer and starting the spring grazing season earlier. In Toluk AA, local
residents reported that small ruminants typically remain on winter pastures until the end of April.
Going forward, livestock should ideally be moved to spring pastures by late March or early April to
reduce the pressure on winter pastures.



32 This is a preliminary planning figure based on national benchmarks. Accurate cost estimation will require technical

surveys, hydrological assessments, and engineering design.
                                                                                                                      91

                                                                                                                 Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework



309.   This is especially relevant for summer pastures, which remain underused. Rotating grazing
plots during the season allows vegetation to recover, improves soil conditions, and increases land
productivity. Positive experience from Naryn oblast, documented by CAMP Alatoo, can serve as a
model for replication in the project areas. This measures will apply to the three AAs (Sary-Kamysh,
Toluk and E. Imanaliev).
310.     The following activities will be implemented in order to revise the grazing calendar.
8.5.1.1 Infrastructure support
311.   To enable the implementation of rotational grazing, necessary infrastructures such as
bridges, roads, or watering points may be required, in pasture areas where there is no access to
water or where water sources are located too far away. The project may also assess broader issues
related to access to water to better understand availability for agriculture and consumption and
how water supply and quality may be improved.
312.    There are also cases where livestock must be moved through areas adjacent to arable and
cultivated lands. To prevent crop damage during these movements, fencing may be needed along
livestock corridors. All these details will be identified and addressed during the planning process.
313.  Such activities will be clarified during the development of the pasture management plan,
which will focus on revising the grazing calendar.
8.5.1.2 Development of pasture management plans
314.    To support an effective adaptation, it is essential to develop a coordinated five-year Pasture
Management Plan in close collaboration with local communities (1 year grazing plan + 5 year
strategy). Its implementation will require full support from both local authorities and the Ministry
of Agriculture. Current government programs, such as concessional loans, cooperative support, and
free seed distribution for pasture restoration, should be effectively leveraged. The active
involvement of institutions responsible for pasture management at the district and national levels
will be key to success
315.   It is also essential to train municipal enterprise staff and pasture specialists in the
development and implementation of five-year pasture management plans. These plans should
include detailed records of pasture users, livestock numbers, pasture conditions, productivity levels,
grazing schedules, infrastructure needs and costs, and annual budgeting. The absence of such
structured planning remains a systemic challenge throughout Kyrgyzstan, particularly after the
transfer of responsibilities from pasture committees to municipal enterprises.
316.   Eight (8) stakeholder workshops will be conducted among main stakeholders (i.e. municipal
enterprise, pastures specialists etc.). The purpose of these workshops will be to plan the
development and validation of these pasture management plans. Local experts
(pasture/agroecology) will conduct these workshops.
8.5.1.3 Monitoring and control of pasture use plan
317.   There is a need to establish a pasture ecosystem monitoring system, including regular
assessments of vegetation conditions, productivity, and grazing pressure. Management plans
should be revised every 2–3 years based on changes in herd size, climate, pasture degradation, and
hydrological conditions.




                                                                                                                   92

                                                                                                              Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework



318.   The pasture use plan will specify which pasture plots are allocated to how many herders,
the number of animals, and the grazing period (from when to when). This plan must be strictly
followed and monitored.
319.   In addition, infrastructure measures outlined in the plan must be properly implemented. If
pasture improvement activities such as seeding are included to restore degraded areas, these too
must be closely monitored to ensure effective implementation.
320.  Monitoring will primarily involve field visits and related transport costs which can be
covered by Municipal Enterprises budgets. Some basic equipment may also be required for the
Municipal Enterprises involved in implementation.
8.5.1.4 Pasture condition assessment
321.   Pasture condition will be assessed to determine the health of pasture plant communities.
This assessment will use the stick method. The grazing stick utilizes simple plant leaf height
measurements. The grazing stick aims to determine herd size, stocking rates and available grazing
days.
8.5.1.5 Conduct seminars for shepherds on the rotational grazing
322.    Rotational grazing is a proven approach to improve pasture and livestock
performance. Rotational grazing, means moving livestock through different sections (paddocks) of
a pasture rather than leaving them on one large area continuously. This allows grazed plants time
to rest and regrow, which in turn leads to healthier pastures and more available feed for animals.
Continuous grazing (animals always on one paddock) often results in selective feeding, where
livestock repeatedly bite off the tastiest plants and avoid others.
323.   A number of seminars will be conducted among affected shepherds to explain the rotational
grazing approach and its benefits. The seminars will be conducted by local specialists.
8.5.2 Artificial Insemination Program
324.     In order to support agricultural productivity and rural livelihoods, it is proposed to enhance
the capacity of veterinary services by developing an artificial insemination program in the 10
affected villages. Artificial insemination is boosting both the quantity and quality of animals being
born. These improved breed are more profitable to sell because they are larger and gain weight
well. In addition, they also produce more milk, bringing in more income that is driving reinvestment
and creating new opportunities for local dairy farmers.
325.    The project will provide artificial insemination kits for local veterinarians in the 10 villages.
There are veterinary specialists in each Aiyl Aimak employed by the local self-government (Ayil
Okmotu). In addition, there are private veterinarians who provide the vet services on a commercial
basis. 30 veterinarians will be trained on artificial insemination techniques. Stock of frozen semen
for cattle, horses and sheep will be provided. 30 cold storage containers will also be provided.
8.5.3 Agro-processing Facility Support in 3 AAs
326.     None of the three AA (Toluk , Sary-Kamysh, and Imanaliev) have fully developed agro-
processing facilities, such as dairy, meat, or canning plants. Even basic infrastructure like grain mills
is rare; only four are reported in the Imanaliev AA . This lack of infrastructure results in the loss of
added value, the outflow of raw materials, and limited local employment opportunities
327.   Dairy products are currently not being processed locally. For example, cheeses and butter
could be produced under a local brand such as "Sary-Kamysh" or "Toluk", promoting them as
                                                                                                                   93

                                                                                                              Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework



ecologically clean products. To support this, it is recommended to provide assistance to an existing
entrepreneur in the village by equipping them with essential tools such as milk collection tanks,
refrigeration units, and other necessary equipment for small-scale dairy processing.
328.    In Toluk, there have also been discussions about the potential for processing local apples,
hawthorn, and other fruits. These could be used to produce value-added products such as jams or
dried fruit chips.
329.   It is important to assess the specific needs and capacities in each community. However, the
focus should be on supporting existing local entrepreneurs, ensuring the initiative is viable,
sustainable, and community-led.
8.6 Proposed Income Restoration Measures
330.   These measures target directly farmers losing pastures. These measures are tailored to
allow herders losing pastures to restore their income at least at pre-project level.
8.6.1 Strengthening Winter Fodder Supply
331.   With the loss of winter pastures, ensuring sufficient fodder reserves is critical. In E. Imanaliev
AA, there is a potential to expand fodder production through improved irrigation. Toluk AA also has
some irrigation potential, albeit limited. In Sary-Kamysh AA, where no irrigated land exists, low-cost
innovations such as hydroponics should be explored.
332.    In each of the three Ayil Aimaks, E. Imanaliev, Toluk, and Sary-Kamysh, demonstration plots
will be established. These plots will be set up in collaboration with the Kyrgyz Agrarian University
on areas ranging from 3 to 5 hectares.
333.   The best-performing and locally adapted fodder crops available in the country will be
planted, and the plots will be used to demonstrate successful fodder production practices under
local conditions. Through these examples, improved techniques for fodder cultivation will be
introduced to local communities.
334.   Additionally, silage and haylage production is not commonly practiced by local residents,
even though there is potential to use corn stalks, as well as straw from wheat and barley.
335.   In this area, the Jalal-Abad Rural Agricultural Advisory Service has already conducted
demonstration days on proper techniques for preparing silage and haylage. To support and scale
up these efforts, appropriate equipment such as hay choppers and other related machinery can be
procured based on a needs assessment. This would help improve fodder preservation practices and
reduce feed shortages.
8.6.2 Agriculture Loans
336.    Agriculture loans will be offered to herders. These loans are intended to develop
livestock/agriculture income-generating activities.
337.   Farmers will be provided with zero-interest repayable loans rather than traditional credit.
These funds could be managed at the Ayil Aimak level and made available to farmers for fodder
production or for improving the efficiency of livestock management (excluding the purchase of
animals). A suitable financial mechanism should be developed to ensure transparency,
accountability, and sustainability. Various models exist and could be adapted to local conditions.




                                                                                                                   94

                                                                                                              Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework



8.6.3 Vocational Training for Pasture Use, Animal Husbandry, etc
338.   There is currently no dedicated vocational training center for herders at the district level.
However, such training courses can be organized through established institutions, such as the
Kyrgyz National Agrarian University named after Skryabin.
339.   Additionally, professional trainers can be contracted from specialized institutions, such as
Kyrgyz Research Institute of Livestock and Pasture (KyrNIIZh), or from experienced NGOs, including
CAMP Alatoo and the Jalal-Abad Rural Agricultural Advisory Service.
340.   These trainings are particularly relevant for staff of Municipal Enterprises as well as active
pasture users who are engaged in livestock management and pasture planning at the local level.
8.6.4 Promote meat breeds of cattle and horses
341.   This activity will be implemented in Sary-Kamysh and Toluk AAs only. Due to the remoteness
of Toluk and Sary-Kamysh Aiyl Aimaks, it is advisable to promote meat breeds of cattle and horses,
which are less demanding in terms of care and more valuable on the market.
342.   First, an analysis the market for beef breeds will be conducted and recommendations will
be proposed. This analysis will be conducted Expert consultant (livestock expert and market/value
chain consultant.
343.   Then, workshop for farmers on breed selection and marketing will be conducted. Finally the
project will develop mechanisms and will procure bulls/stallions. Purchase of breeding bulls (meat
breed) (1 bull per village) will also be provided.
344.   It is not feasible to purchase a bull or stallion for every affected household. Instead, the
approach will be to provide one bull and one stallion per village, which can be used collectively by
groups of households.
345.  A good example of such a mechanism was observed where a group of 10 households jointly
used one bull for two years. After that period, the bull was sold and the proceeds were used to
purchase two new bulls, one of which was then transferred to another group of 10 households. This
model promotes resource sharing and sustainability.
346.  To ensure transparency and proper use, the mechanism should be coordinated and
monitored by the Ayil Okmotu or the Municipal Enterprise, with clear record-keeping and
agreements in place among beneficiaries.
8.6.5 Development of Goat Farming
347.   In areas with steep terrain and poor vegetation, particularly near the Naryn River, traditional
grazing is limited. In Sary-Kamysh AA, some residents have already begun increasing their goat
herds, which is a promising adaptation strategy.
348.    The goat farming initiative in Sary-Kamysh and Toluk targets a total of 10 herders, five from
each Ayil Aimak. Around The project will purchase 100 cashmere goats (50 in each AA) and will also
purchase 4 breeding buck (2 in each AA). Farmers will be trained on cashmere collection. Support
for local production (equipment, training, packaging, branding) will also be provided.
349.    The goats will be provided to individual herders who already own goats, are familiar with
basic cashmere production techniques, and are committed to seriously engaging in the cashmere
value chain. Preference will be given to affected households and those demonstrating a strong
interest and potential to scale up cashmere production.

                                                                                                                   95

                                                                                                              Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework



350.    In addition, training sessions will be organized on high-quality cashmere harvesting and
basic processing, including the development of value-added products. These trainings will involve
active farmers, including women engaged in felt-making and other related crafts, to encourage
inclusive participation and promote local entrepreneurship.
351.   This approach is expected to serve as a demonstration model, encouraging wider interest in
goat farming. Those who receive goats will be expected to share their knowledge and experience,
and clear responsibilities and mutual obligations will be formalized at the Ayil Okmotu level.
8.6.6 Development of Yak Farming
352.    Yaks are another viable option: they are resilient to cold, require minimal supervision, and
are well-suited for remote summer pastures.
353.    The project will purchase 60 yaks (20 in each AA). The project will also provide winter shelter,
veterinary services, feed and fooders. Farmers will be trained on cashmere collection. Support for
local production (equipment, training, packaging, branding) will also be provided.
354.    Each selected household will be granted 30 yaks, with a total of 30 households supported
across both Ayil Okmotu areas. These households will receive specialized training similar to that
provided under the cashmere goat initiative, focusing on yak husbandry, breeding, feeding, and
basic product development (e.g., fibre, dairy, meat).
355.   The trainings will be open to all interested community members, not limited to the direct
beneficiaries. This approach is intended to encourage broader community engagement and
promote the wider adoption of improved yak farming practices.
8.6.7 Vocational Training Related to Construction Activities
356.   Vocational training will also be offered in field related to K1-HPP construction activities. The
following training will be offered: 1) welding, 2) Crane Operator, 3) Concrete Worker, 4) Heavy
Equipment Operator, 5) Excavator Operator / Loader Operator.
357.   Training will be conducted in Bishkek. Travel and accommodation in Bishkek will be covered
under the Project.
8.7 Implementation Arrangements for the livelihood restoration activities as part of the
    LRRP
358.  The K1-PMO under the Ministry of Energy of Kyrgyzstan, will be responsible for the
implementation of the LRRP.
359.   The PMO will hire a Livelihood Program Manager to manage the overall implementation of
the LRRP. The Livelihood program manager will be supported by 3 Livelihood Field Officers (one for
each AA: Sary-Kamysh, Toluk and E. Imanaliev).
360.    The Program Manager will manage the livelihood restoration elements of the LRRP. The
main tasks of the Program Manager will be to develop a plan for livelihood restoration and its
implementation, coordinate efforts done by Ministry of Energy and other entities, identification of
PAP’s needs and development of additional livelihood restoration measures as may be required,
and monitoring of livelihood restoration measures in collaboration with the monitoring and
evaluation specialist.” Among other qualifications, the Program Manager will have a background in
employment generation, livelihood restoration, skills development, vocational training, or a related
field and demonstrated experience managing multi-faceted development programs.

                                                                                                                   96

                                                                                                              Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program   Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework



361.    This person will be supported in his/her work by three (3) Livelihood Field Officers, which
will be based in the three (3) AA (one for each AA: Sary-Kamysh, Toluk and E. Imanaliev).
362.    These Livelihood Field Officers will work more proactively and interactively with affected
families. Livelihood Field Officers will have experience working at the community level on
community development, counseling, vocational training, entrepreneurship development,
agricultural extension, and/or related fields. They will have strong community engagement skills.
8.8     Timetable / workplan for LRRP Implementation
363.   This LRRP will be revised during the preparation of the LRRP for the inundated area
(expected 2028). The LRRP will be implemented over a 3 years period.
364.    The K1-PMO will begin implementation of the LRRP as soon as the LRRP for the inundated
area is adopted by the GoKR.

9 ASSISTANCE TO VULNERABLE PEOPLE
9.1 Identification of Vulnerable Groups
367.    Disadvantaged/vulnerable individuals and groups may be affected disproportionately,
either adversely or positively, by the project. Because of low socioeconomic profile, their voices
may not be heard in the formal decision-making process, or included in consultation process but
their feedback are not solicited in a manner that offers sufficient understanding of how they are
affected, how they would respond, and what support they may need to avoid or mitigate such
impact from their own perspective.
368.     Identification of vulnerable individuals and groups, is an important step to effectively
engaging them in the consultation process throughout the project cycle. This enables project
implementing agency (MoE through the K1-PMO) to work out measures to avoid adverse impacts,
or minimize or mitigate such impacts to vulnerable groups, if avoidance is not possible. Inclusion of
disadvantaged/vulnerable individuals and groups, at the early stage of project `planning, can also
assist in project design that provide them opportunities to restore and improve their livelihoods.
369.   Vulnerable groups under the project may include the following categories, among others: 1)
Low-income households and individuals; 2) The elderly ; 3) People with disabilities; 4) Women
headed low-income households.
370.    Based on SES to be conducted during LRRPs preparation, the categories of vulnerable HHs
will be reconfirmed under the project.
9.1.1 Low-income HHs
371.   As indicated in Chapter 2, the poverty rate is in the 4 Aiyl Aimaks is very high, respectively
27.2% in E. Imanaliev AA, 39.6% in Sary-Kamysh AA, 41.7% in Atai AA and 42.9% in Toluk AA. In Sary-
Kamysh and E. Imanaliev a high number of families are living in extreme poverty (see Table 4)
372.    The number of low income HHs affected by the project (i.e. losing pastures of other assets)
is not yet known. However, due to the high number of low income HHs located in the 4 AA, it is
expected that a significant number of PAPs will belong to the low-income HHs category. The SES to
be conducted during LRRP2 preparation will identify the number of low income HH among the PAPs.
373.   Low-income households receive monthly welfare payments in accordance with the Law "On
the Fundamentals of Social Services to the Population in the Kyrgyz Republic “. In particular, low-
income families were given the opportunity to enter into social contracts with the government.
Under this program, each household received a non-repayable financial grant of 100,000 KGS.
9.1.2 People with disabilities
374.    Table 5 provided information on the number of persons with disabilities (PWD) by AAs. The
data is divided into two age groups: children under 18 and adults over 18. Among the three AAs,
there are a total of 804 individuals with disabilities: 262 are under 18 years old, and 542 are 18
years and older. Notably, E. Imanaliev AA has the highest number of persons with disabilities, which
may be attributed to its larger population size. PWD receive social assistance from the Government.
375.  Out of the 337 surveyed households, 51 households include disabled persons. The SES to be
conducted during LRRP preparation will identify the number of PWD among the PAPs.
376.  PWD also receive monthly welfare payments in accordance with the Law "On the
Fundamentals of Social Services to the Population in the Kyrgyz Republic “

                                                                                                                   99

                                                                                                              Official Use Only
Kambarata-1 HPP: Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program      Draft Resettlement & Livelihood Restoration Framework



9.1.3 Women-headed Households
377.   According to the National Statistical Committee (NSC) of the Kyrgyz Republic, the
percentage of households with female heads was approximately 36.7% in 2017. However, based on
the households survey conducted in the project area, out of the 337 surveyed households, only 4
(1.2%) have been identified as female-headed households in the project area. The SES to be
conducted during LRRP2 preparation will identify the number of affected households with female
head.
378.    According to an ADB report33, households with female heads are among those most
susceptible to poverty. The increasing trend of households headed by females due to the high level
of male labor migration has also intensified women’s time poverty. Reports from some women’s
NGOs indicate that many of them face problems related to housing, living conditions, and limited
access to natural resources such as lack of heating, lighting, fuel, safe sanitation, and poor quality
of drinking water that leads to malnutrition and diseases. This increases the time and work burden
of rural women and children who are responsible for collecting water, and negatively reduces
women’s potential opportunities for income-generating activities.
9.1.4 Elderly
379.    Elderly are households with members aged 60 and above in need of care and assistance. In
Kyrgyzstan, the social insurance coverage is almost universal among the elderly. Old age pension is
existing in Kyrgyzstan. As well as a basic pension, a social pension exists for elderly individuals who
did not contribute to the social insurance system.
380.   According to a survey conducted34, older women and men in Kyrgyzstan reported that they
experience discrimination on the basis of their age in different aspects of their lives, including in
employment, access to financial services and access to essential goods. They are subject to violence
and abuse, with over a third of respondents reporting that they had experienced at least one type
of personal crime, violence or abuse since the age of 50. In the vast majority of cases, this appeared
to happen with impunity and little access to redress.
9.2 Social Safety Nets Programs
381.    The social assistance system is centered on providing support to groups identified as needy
or vulnerable, including low-income families with children, the elderly, the disabled, families living
in high altitude or remote areas.
382.   According to a report prepared by the International Monetary Fund35, social assistance
spending as a share of GDP remained broadly stable over the last ten years, at 1.7 percent of GDP
on average.
383.    The two main social assistance schemes are the monthly Social Benefit (SB) and the monthly
Benefit for Low-Income Families with Children (BLFC). In 2021, spending on these two programs
accounted for 35 and 32 percent of the total social assistance spending (0.6 and 0.5 percent of GDP).
The BLFC is the largest program in terms of the number of beneficiaries, with 5.3 percent of the
total population in 2021 who benefited from BLFC compared to 1.5 percent for other programs.
384.  The third largest program is the Monetary Compensation (MC) scheme which accounted for
15 percent of the social assistance spending in 2021 (about 0.25 percent of GDP). All social

33

   HelpAge International (2013) The rights of older people in Kyrgyzstan
35

assistance programs are administered by the Ministry of Labor, Social Security and Migration and
financed out of the general government budget.
9.3 Remote villages
385.   The villages of Sary-Kamysh AA: Birlik, Kotormo, Imanaliev AA: Nichke-Sai, Chorgochu, Toluk
AA: Toluk, and Chaar-Tash are officially designated as remote and hard-to-reach (Government
Resolution No. 377 dated June 25, 1997, with amendments made in resolution No. 504, dated
September 9, 2013 ), granting residents access to several benefits:
         -    Salary bonuses: Regional coefficients ranging from 1.15 to 1.45
         -    50 percent discount on travel (there and back) once a year for persons traveling for
              medical consultation and treatment to other regions of the Kyrgyz Republic, if such
              services are not available at the place of residence;
         -    The right to free travel on public transport (except taxis) once a year (there and back)
              for children registered in medical and preventive institutions
9.4 Risks Associated with Vulnerable Groups
386.    It is particularly important to understand whether project impacts may disproportionately
affect socially vulnerable populations, who are often not in a position to communicate their
concerns or understand the impacts of the project. Vulnerable status may be due to origin, gender,
age, health status, economic difficulties, financial insecurity, socially disadvantaged position in the
community , dependence on others (dependency) or on natural resources, and etc.
387.    Interaction with vulnerable groups and individuals often requires special measures and
assistance to support their participation in the project-related decision making, so that their
awareness and contributions to the overall process are comparable with those of other
stakeholders.
388.      Risks associated with vulnerable groups are summarized in the table below.

9.5 Strategy to incorporate views of vulnerable social groups

389.   Socially vulnerable population groups will be consulted with using special methods, if
necessary. Methods of interaction that will be used in the Project are described in the sections
below.
390.      The website and other social media of the Ministry of Energy will be used for disclosing
project documentation, including SEP/LRRPs, project updates and information, as well as the
Project GM in Russian/Kyrgyz language. Where Internet use is not feasible, traditional methods
such as face-to-face meetings, focus groups, information stands and distribution of booklets
will be used to inform and engage vulnerable groups. Further consultations to determine the
final list of vulnerable groups as part of the social impact assessment and LRRPs preparation will
be conducted in the course of the Project implementation.
391.    As indicated in the SEP, the Project will take special measures to ensure that vulnerable
social groups have equal opportunity to be involved in project activities, access information and
benefits of the project, provide feedback, or submit grievances. The following mechanisms will
be used for disclosure of information to vulnerable groups:
   a) Social networks and traditional media for any announcements related to the project.
      These include the official web pages of the ministries, as well as social media
      (Facebook, Instagram, Telegram) and official newspapers.
   b) Publication of all information about the project, including the GM process, in
      Russian/Kyrgyz;
   c) In the case of public meetings, including workshops or meetings, these meetings
      should be held at a suitable time and place for women, and should also take into
      account the needs of persons with disabilities (if any), or the barriers faced by socially
      vulnerable population groups. These meetings are often more conveniently held in
      public spaces or in women's civil society organizations (if available).

9.6 Specific Support to Vulnerable Groups

9.6.1 Involvement in Livelihood Restoration program
393.  Vulnerable HHs among PAPs will be entitled to participate in the Livelihood Restoration
Program. For vulnerable households, there is an opportunity within the project to contribute to
improving their living standards.

9.6.2 Additional support to Vulnerable Groups
394.   In addition to compensation for lost assets, additional support will be provided to
vulnerable HHs. All vulnerable PAPs will receive an allowance corresponding to social welfare
payments for 1 year.

10 IMPLEMENTATION ARRANGEMENTS
395.    For the implementation of the K1-HPP, the Ministry of Energy of the Kyrgyz Republic has
established a Project Management Office (K1-PMO). The K1-PMO is located in Bishkek and is
headed by a Project Manager.
396.   K1-PMO is responsible for assigning appropriate staff and budget, sufficient to
implement this LRRF and prepare and implement, as needed, the LRRPs and update/complete
the LRRP. Qualified consultants may be mobilized to assist K1-PMO in development of the
LRRPs. The LRRPs implementation will require the participation of national, regional and local
agencies and organizations.
10.1 Central Level
10.1.1 Ministry of Energy (MoE)
397.   MoE will provide guidance on overall project implementation and coordinate with other
government agencies as needed to facilitate project implementation. MoE tasks are the
followings:
       a.   Provide overall management and coordination for the K1-HPP;
       b.   Approve the LRRF/LRRPs
       c.   Establish the cut-off dates
       d.   Ensure timely compensation to persons for acquired assets;
       e.   Concludes agreements with PAPs on the compensation of land and other assets;
       f.   Acts as the verificatory of cadastral works for the acquisition of land and non-land
            assets, including clarification of the boundaries of acquired land;
       g.   Directly finance activities to relocate PAPs losing houses and their families in the
            event of their resettlement to new locations;
10.1.2 Ministry of Finance (MoF)
398.     MoF tasks are the followings:
       a.     Development and implementation of state policy in the field of public finance
              management.
       b.     Allocation of funds for the compensation/support of lost assets related to land
              acquisition, land use restriction, involuntary resettlement and restoration of
              livelihoods.
10.1.3 Ministry of Emergency Situations (MoES)
399.     MoEm tasks are the followings
       a.    Approve decisions on the allocation of land plots for the construction of
             residential, administrative, and industrial buildings and structures to prevent the
             allocation of land in areas prone to hazardous natural processes.
10.1.4 Kambarata 1 Project Management Office (K1-PMO)
400.   The K1- PMO will play the overall coordination roles and taking the lead responsibilities
on land acquisition, compensation and support, livelihood restoration, procurement
management, financial management (FM) and disbursement, E&S documentation, and
compliance. Detailed tasks are listed below:

         a.     Ensure preparation and implementation of LRRPs;
         b.     Request information on existing land titles/contracts on land and non-land assets
